Key Takeaway
Logic discusses words to facilitate communication, and when defining, one should prefer essential definitions (ḥadd) over descriptive ones (rasm) when possible.

Definition of Indication and Its Divisions in Islamic Logic
Author defines indication and divides it into verbal/non-verbal and conventional/natural.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of indication (dal, madlul, dalala) and classify any given indicator as verbal/non-verbal and conventional/natural based on the conditions set by the author.

Core Idea
Indication is the key that unlocks knowledge from signs, both verbal and natural.

In this passage, the author lays the foundational concept of dalala, which is central to all Islamic logic and linguistic sciences. Understanding this definition and its divisions is the first step in grasping how meanings are conveyed. We will break down the definition into its elements (indicator, indicated, and the relation) and then learn to sort indications into their proper categories.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of dalala
Find the Arabic phrase 'كون الشيء بحيث يلزم من العلم به العلم بشيء آخر' which defines indication as a state of a thing such that knowledge of it entails knowledge of another thing.
2
Identify the three components
From the definition, identify the indicator (الدال: the first thing known), the indicated (المدلول: the second thing known), and indication (الدلالة: the property connecting them).
3
Distinguish verbal vs. non-verbal
Note the conditional: if the indicator is a word, indication is verbal; otherwise non-verbal. For example, the word 'Zayd' is verbal, while a nod is non-verbal.
4
Distinguish conventional vs. natural
Examine the cause: if the connection is due to a positer's setting (وضع), it is conventional (e.g., 'Zayd' indicates Zayd by convention); if due to natural requirement (طبع), it is natural (e.g., a groan indicates pain naturally).
5
Apply the classification to a real Islamic example
Take the adhan (call to prayer): it is verbal (words) and conventional (based on Shari'ah positing). Thus, it is verbal conventional indication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلذا بدأ بذكر الدلالة، وهي كون الشيء بحيث يلزم من العلم به العلم بشيء آخر، والأول هو الدال، والثاني هو المدلول، والدال إن كان لفظا فالدلالة "لفظية"، وإلا فغير لفظية". وكل منهما إن كان بسبب وضع الواضع وتعيينه الأول بإزاء الثاني "فوضعية"، كدلالة لفظ "زيد" على ذاته، ودلالة الدوال الأربع على مدلولاتها، وإن كان بسبب اقتضاء الطبع حدوث الدال عند عروض المدلول "فطبعية"، كدلالة "أح أح"
So he began by mentioning indication, and it is the state of a thing such that knowledge of it entails knowledge of another thing. The first is the indicator, the second is the indicated. If the indicator is a word, then the indication is 'verbal', otherwise it is 'non-verbal'. Each of these two, if it is due to the setting and determination of a positer of the first in exchange for the second, is 'conventional', like the indication of the word 'Zayd' to his essence, and the indication of the four indicators to their meanings; and if it is due to the requirement of nature that the indicator occurs upon the occurrence of the indicated, then it is 'natural', like the indication of 'ah ah' (a groan).
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage provides a general definition of indication (dalala) as the state where knowledge of one thing entails knowledge of another. The indicator (dal) and indicated (madlul) are introduced. Indication is then divided: if the indicator is a word, it is verbal; otherwise non-verbal. Both can be further divided into conventional (wad'iyya) and natural (tabi'iyya). Conventional indication is due to a conscious positing, e.g., the word 'Zayd' indicates Zayd himself. Natural indication arises from a natural connection, e.g., a groan indicates pain. The 'four indicators' refer to knots, posts, lines, and gestures, which are conventional signs.

Key Takeaway
Indication (dalala) is defined as the property of a thing that, when known, leads to knowledge of another, and it is divided into verbal/non-verbal and conventional/natural depending on the nature of the indicator and the cause of the connection.

Definition and Six Divisions of Indication
Author defines indication and classifies it into six types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define indication (dalala) and classify any given indication into its six types based on medium (linguistic/non-linguistic) and cause (conventional/natural/rational).

Core Idea
Indication is necessary knowledge from signifier to signified; it divides into six precise types.

Indication is the foundation of all communication and inference in Islamic logic. This lesson unpacks the definition of dalala and its six-fold classification, which will help you analyze how signs—whether words, natural symptoms, or rational clues—convey meaning. We will see how the author sets up the framework for the entire discussion of terms.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of indication
Identify the key components: the signifier (dal), the signified (madlul), and the necessary relation (luzum). Notice that the definition is epistemological: it goes from knowledge to knowledge.
2
Distinguish linguistic vs non-linguistic indication
Check whether the signifier is a word or not. This is the first branching criterion.
3
Distinguish conventional, natural, and rational indication
These are sub-types based on what causes the relation. Conventional depends on assignment by a positor (wad'), natural depends on natural concomitance (tab'), rational depends on pure reason ('aql).
4
Apply the classification to examples
For any given example, first ask: is the signifier a word? Then ask: what causes the relation? Combine to get one of six types.
5
Practice with Islamic examples
For instance, the word 'Allah' refers to the Creator by convention (linguistic conventional). A fever indicating illness is natural non-linguistic. Smoke indicating fire is rational non-linguistic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالبحث عن الألفاظ حيث الإفادة والاستفادة، وهما إنما تكونان بالدلالة؛ فلذا بدأ بذكر الدلالة، وهي كون الشيء بحيث يلزم من العلم به العلم بشيء آخر، والأول هو الدال، والثاني هو المدلول، والدال إن كان لفظا فالدلالة 'لفظية'، وإلا فغير لفظية. وكل منهما إن كان بسبب وضع الواضع وتعيينه الأول بإزاء الثاني 'فوضعية'، كدلالة لفظ 'زيد' على ذاته، ودلالة الدوال الأربع على مدلولاتها، وإن كان بسبب اقتضاء الطبع حدوث الدال عند عروض المدلول 'فطبعية'، كدلالة 'أح أح' على وجع الصدر ودلالة سرعة النبض على الحمى، وإن كان بسبب أمر غير الوضع والطبع، فالدلالة 'عقلية'، كدلالة لفظ 'ديز' المسموع من وراء الجدار على وجود اللافظ، وكدلالة الدخان على النار. فأقسام الدلالة ستة.
Then the discussion of words is where communication and understanding occur, and they only happen through indication; therefore, the author began by mentioning indication, which is that a thing is such that from knowledge of it, knowledge of another thing necessarily follows. The first is the signifier, and the second is the signified. If the signifier is a word, then the indication is 'linguistic'; otherwise, it is 'non-linguistic'. Each of these, if it is due to the convention of a positor and his assigning the first in exchange for the second, then it is 'conventional', like the indication of the word 'Zayd' for his essence, and the indication of the four signs for their significations. If it is due to the requirement of nature that the signifier occurs upon the occurrence of the signified, then it is 'natural', like the indication of 'Ah ah' for chest pain and the indication of a rapid pulse for fever. If it is due to something other than convention and nature, then the indication is 'rational', like the indication of the word 'diz' heard from behind a wall for the existence of the speaker, and like the indication of smoke for fire. So the divisions of indication are six.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage defines the central concept of 'indication' (dalala) and then classifies it into six types. The definition is epistemological: indication is a relation of necessary knowledge from one thing (the signifier) to another (the signified). The classification proceeds by two criteria: the medium (linguistic or non-linguistic) and the cause (conventional, natural, or rational). Linguistic indication uses words; non-linguistic uses other signs. Conventional indication depends on human agreement (e.g., proper names or traffic signs). Natural indication relies on a natural causal connection (e.g., a groan indicates pain). Rational indication involves logical inference, as when hearing a sound from behind a wall indicates the presence of a speaker, or smoke indicates fire. The author emphasizes that these categories are exhaustive, yielding exactly six divisions.

الدال
المدلول
دلالة لفظية

دلالة طبعية
دلالة عقلية

Key Takeaway
Indication is the basis of all communication; its six types cover all ways we gain knowledge from signs.

Three Types of Linguistic Conventional Indication
Author lists the three subdivisions of linguistic conventional indication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish mutabaqa, tadammun, and iltizam in any given word-meaning relationship.

Core Idea
A single word can indicate its meaning in three distinct ways: whole, part, or necessary accompaniment.

Logic primarily deals with linguistic conventional indication (al-dalala al-lafziyya al-wad'iyya) because it is the foundation of communication. The author subdivides this indication into three types: conformity (mutabaqa), inclusion (tadammun), and implication (iltizam). Understanding these three allows you to analyse how words convey meaning in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the word and its primary meaning
Take a word like 'house' (bayt). Its primary meaning is a building with rooms. What does it signify?
The word must be conventional (wad'i) for this analysis.
2
Check for mutabaqa (conformity)
Does the word indicate the entire meaning? The word 'house' indicates the whole building. This is mutabaqa: the word matches its complete meaning.
Mutabaqa is the default type.
3
Check for tadammun (inclusion)
Does the word indicate a part of its meaning? 'House' indicates the door, because the door is part of the building. This is tadammun: the word includes a part.
The part must be inherent in the meaning.
4
Check for iltizam (implication)
Does the word indicate something outside but necessarily connected? 'House' indicates the inhabitant, because a house implies an inhabitant. This is iltizam.
The connection must be necessary in the mind.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
For the word 'salat' (prayer): mutabaqa indicates the entire prayer act; tadammun indicates the recitation (a part); iltizam indicates that the performer is a Muslim.
Practice with other fiqh terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقصود بالبحث ههنا الدلالة اللفظية الوضعية؛ إذ عليها مدار الإفادة والاستفادة وهي تنقسم إلى مطابقة وتضمن والتزام. فإن قلت: إنه يلزم على هذا التقدير دخول دلالة سرعة النبض على الحمى أيضًا تحت الدلالة العقلية؛ لكون هذه الدلالة من قبيل دلالة الأثر على المؤثر، فبطلت الطبعية غير اللفظية، وانحصرت أقسام الدلالة في الخمسة. قلت: لا مضايقة في اجتماع الدلالتين باعتبارين مختلفين، فدلالة سرعة النبض من حيث أنه أثر على الحمى، ومن حيث أنه مؤثر باعتبار العلاقة الذاتية 'دلالة عقلية'، ومن حيث أنه ظهر سرعة النبض بحسب إظهار الطبعية عند عروض الحمى 'دلالة' طبعية، فثبت وجودها، وصارت الأقسام ستة، وهذا هو الحق.
And the intended focus here is linguistic conventional indication, because communication and understanding revolve around it, and it is divided into conformity, inclusion, and implication. If you say: Then it necessarily follows from this estimation that the indication of a rapid pulse for fever also enters under rational indication, because this indication is of the type of indication of an effect upon the cause; thus the non-linguistic natural indication becomes invalid, and the divisions of indication are confined to five. I say: There is no difficulty in the combination of the two indications from two different aspects. So the indication of a rapid pulse, in that it is an effect upon the fever, and in that it is a cause (as a cause, according to the essential relation), is 'rational indication', and in that the rapid pulse appeared according to the manifestation of nature upon the occurrence of the fever, it is 'natural indication'. Thus its existence is established, and the divisions become six, and this is the truth.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage first specifies that the relevant indication for logical discourse is linguistic conventional indication (dalala lafziyya wad'iyya), because it is the foundation of communication. This indication is then subdivided into three: mutabaqa (conformity, where the word indicates the entire meaning), tadammun (inclusion, where it indicates a part of the meaning), and iltizam (implication, where it indicates something accompanying the meaning). The author then addresses an objection regarding the classification of natural indication. The objection argues that rapid pulse indicating fever is actually rational indication (cause-effect), thus nullifying natural indication and reducing the six divisions to five. The response clarifies that a single sign can be considered from two aspects: as an effect (rational) and as a natural occurrence (natural). Thus the six-fold division is preserved. This is typical of the author's method of preemptively resolving classification issues.

مطابقة
تضمن
التزام

A) Mutabaqa
B) Tadammun
C) Iltizam
D) Natural indication

Key Takeaway
Linguistic conventional indication has three types: conformity (mutabaqa) for the whole meaning, inclusion (tadammun) for a part, and implication (iltizam) for a necessary accompaniment.

Preserving the Six-Fold Division of Indication
Author defends the six-fold division against an objection by showing dual perspective.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the same sign can be both rationally and naturally indicative from different perspectives, preserving the six-fold division of indication.

Core Idea
A single sign like a rapid pulse can be both rational and natural indication depending on the aspect considered.

The author previously stated that the intended indication is linguistic conventional, but he also recognizes other types: rational, natural, and three subdivisions. An objection arises: if a rapid pulse indicates fever rationally (as effect), then natural indication is redundant. The author resolves this by showing that the same sign can be viewed from two distinct aspects, thereby preserving all six divisions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector argues: The rapid pulse indicating fever is an effect indicating its cause, which is rational indication. So natural indication (pulse naturally accompanying fever) is not needed. Thus the divisions reduce to five.
The objection assumes that one sign cannot belong to two types simultaneously.
2
Grasp the response's key concept
The author responds that 'there is no difficulty in the combination of the two indications from two different aspects.' This means the same sign can have multiple aspects.
Aspect (i'tibar) is crucial: we consider the sign under different relations.
3
Apply the dual perspective to the example
Rapid pulse: as an effect of fever → rational indication. As a natural occurrence that appears due to fever → natural indication. Both coexist without conflict.
The sign itself is one, but the relation differs.
4
Conclude the six-fold division
Thus natural indication remains valid alongside rational, giving three types of non-linguistic indication (rational, natural, and also possibly conventional non-linguistic) and three types of linguistic conventional. Total six.
The author affirms that this is the truth.

Source Text

The Six Types of Indication and Focus on Verbal Conventional
Author classifies indication into six types and justifies why verbal conventional indication is the subject of logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify indication into its six types and justify why logicians focus exclusively on verbal conventional indication.

Core Idea
Indication is the bridge from known to unknown; verbal conventional indication is the most universal and precise tool for knowledge exchange.

This passage introduces the foundational concept of indication (dalala) — how one thing points to another. The author categorises indication into six types, distinguishing rational, natural, and conventional. He then argues that logic only treats verbal conventional indication because it alone enables easy and comprehensive communication. In this lesson, you will learn to navigate this classification and understand the logician's rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three main causal categories
Read the opening where the author mentions indication based on convention (wad'), nature (tabi'), and reason ('aql). Note that each category is defined by the source of the relationship: human agreement, natural cause, or rational inference.
The actual six types derive from subdividing rational indication into three subtypes.
2
Distinguish rational from natural indication via the pulse example
Examine the example of rapid pulse indicating fever. The author shows that the same phenomenon can be both a rational indication (as an effect indicating its cause) and a natural indication (as a natural symptom). Understand how one thing can have dual indication under different aspects.
This resolves the objection that would reduce six types to five.
3
Locate the objection and response preserving the six-fold division
Find where al-Sayyid al-Sharif argues that the pulse example makes natural indication redundant, collapsing the count to five. The author responds that the two aspects coexist, so both rational and natural remain distinct.
The commentator Isma'il supports this with the example of a horse running upon seeing fodder.
4
Extract the justification for focusing on verbal conventional indication
Read the section beginning with 'The purpose: It is as if the questioner asks...' The author argues that other forms (gestures, writing, contracts) are either limited or require tools, while speech is universally accessible and efficient.
This justification establishes why logic, concerned with giving and seeking benefit, centers on verbal conventional indication.

The conceptions. The purpose of the divisions of indication: indication of chest pain and indication of rapid pulse upon fever, even if due to something other than convention and nature, then the indication is 'rational', like the indication of the word 'sound' heard from behind the wall upon the existence of the speaker, and like the indication of smoke upon fire. So the divisions of indication are six, and the purpose of investigation here is verbal conventional indication, for upon it revolve giving and seeking benefit, and it is divided into correspondence, inclusion, and implication. As for rational indication: know that in rational indication a self-relationship is necessary, i.e., a relationship of effect; it includes the indication of the effect upon the cause, like the indication of smoke upon fire, and the indication of the cause upon the effect, like the indication of fire upon smoke, and the indication of one effect upon another, like the indication of smoke upon heat, for they are two effects of fire. If you say: This estimation necessitates the inclusion of the indication of rapid pulse upon fever under rational indication as well, because this indication is of the type of indication of effect upon cause; thus the natural non-verbal indication becomes invalid, and the divisions of indication are confined to five; and therefore al-Sayyid al-Sind al-Sharif (may his secret be sanctified) denied its existence. I say: There is no harm in the combination of the two indications under different aspects; for the indication of rapid pulse, insofar as it is an effect upon fever and insofar as it is a cause according to the self-relationship, is 'rational indication', and insofar as it appears by way of natural manifestation upon the occurrence of fever, it is 'natural indication'. Thus its existence is established, and the divisions become six. This is the truth, and to it went al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani. The most correct in exemplifying this indication is the running of an animal upon seeing fodder, for nothing is objected to it. (Isma'il) 'Like the indication': He did not say the word 'Zayd' so that the example would be exclusively for the thing exemplified without admixture of another. 'From behind the wall': He restricted it thus, because if it were heard from sight, the existence of the speaker would be known by sight. And the purpose: It is as if the questioner asks: Why did the author restrict himself to mentioning verbal conventional indication, dividing it into correspondence, inclusion, and implication, and turn away from the other divisions? So he answered: The investigation of words is only for giving and seeking benefit, and both are obtained from verbal conventional indication, so it is the purpose in this context, and its divisions are necessary, and there is no need for others. (Burhan) 'Upon it': For if you say: Benefit can be given and sought by other than verbal conventional indication, through signs, lines, contracts, installations, illumination, natural and rational indication. I say: Signs do not reach non-existent things; lines require instruments; contracts and installations are not common understanding so that what is in the mind can be known through them; illumination is specific to illuminationists, while speech is specific to us; natures are different; and the indication of effect may be ambiguous and hidden. So the purpose is not obtained by natural and rational indication either. Thus no indication remains easier and more comprehensive except verbal conventional indication; therefore he considered it. (Isma'il with addition) It is

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the types of indication (dalala) and an argument for why logic focuses on verbal conventional indication. The author begins by listing six types: natural, conventional, rational, etc., but the discussion centers on the distinction between rational (aqliyya) and natural (tabi'iyya) indication. Using the example of rapid pulse indicating fever, the author shows that an indication can be both rational (as effect on cause) and natural (as a natural symptom) simultaneously, thus preserving the six-fold division. Then the author addresses why only verbal conventional indication is treated in logic: it is the easiest and most comprehensive for conveying meaning. The commentary by Isma'il and Burhan expands on the examples and justification, arguing that other forms of indication (gestures, writing, etc.) are either limited or require external tools, whereas speech is universally accessible.

Indication types are distinguished by their causal basis; verbal conventional indication is preferred for logic because it alone enables universal and effortless communication of meanings.

What Is Mantiq?

Three Types of Verbal Conventional Indication: Mutabaqa, Tadamun, Iltizam
Author defines three types of verbal conventional indication and condition for iltizam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given verbal conventional indication as mutabaqa, tadamun, or iltizam, and explain why iltizam requires a mental relationship (rational or customary).

Core Idea
Verbal indication covers the whole meaning, a part, or an external necessary concept.

This lesson unpacks the three types of verbal conventional indication: mutabaqa (correspondence), tadamun (inclusion), and iltizam (implication). You will learn how to differentiate them and why iltizam requires a mental relationship. Using the example of 'blindness' and its relationship to sight, the passage clarifies a critical nuance between inclusion and implication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Notice the author lists three modes: indication on the entirety (tamam), part (juz'), or external matter (amr kharaj). These correspond to mutabaqa, tadamun, and iltizam respectively.
2
Understand the condition for iltizam
Iltizam requires a mental relationship (luzum dhihni) – either rational (aqli) or customary (urfi). The external matter must be such that the thing signified cannot be conceived without it.
Rational: mind judges impossibility; Customary: custom makes it inseparable.
3
Analyze the blindness example
Blindness is defined as 'absence of sight in something capable of sight.' Sight is external to this concept because it is the annexed-to (mudaf ilayh). Hence, the indication of 'blindness' on sight is implicative (iltizam), not inclusive (tadamun).
The author refutes the objection that sight is part of the meaning by showing that the annexed-to is external.
4
Distinguish tamam from jami'
The author chooses 'tamam' (completeness) over 'jami'' (totality) because 'jami'' suggests composition (tarkib) and requires encompassing multiplicity, while 'tamam' is opposite of deficiency and does not imply composition.
This clarifies the precision of definitions in logic.

النبي)
The divisions of indication: and a mental relationship is necessary in it, either rational or customary, because the indication of a word due to the setting of the language setter is either upon the entirety of the thing signified, or upon part of it, or upon an external matter. His saying: 'and a mental relationship is necessary in it' means in the indication of implication. His saying: 'of the relationship' means that the external matter is such that it is impossible to conceive the thing signified without it, whether this mental relationship is rational like sight in relation to blindness, or... (He said): 'Upon the entirety' - The author chose the word 'completeness' (tamam) instead of 'totality' (jami') because the latter suggests composition unlike the former, even though the indication of a single word upon its meaning is also correspondence. The difference between them is that completeness does not require encompassing multiplicity potentially or actually, thus the Necessary is said to be the perfection of existence unlike totality, for it requires encompassing multiplicity. So completeness is opposite to deficiency, and totality is opposite to partiality. This is understood from the words of the Shaykh in al-Shifa'. Know that the restriction of verbal conventional indication to correspondence, inclusion, and implication is rational; for if it is not a matter of negation and affirmation, it reverts to it by saying: The indication is either upon the entirety of the thing signified or not; the first is correspondence; the second is either an indication upon part of the thing signified or not; the first is inclusion; the second is implication. This mental relationship indicates that it is of two types: rational and customary; for the impossibility of conceiving the thing signified without the external matter is either by virtue of the mind, such that the mind judges the impossibility of that conception, then the mental relationship is rational; or by virtue of custom and usual practice, such that the mind allows conceiving the thing signified without the external matter but according to custom that conception is impossible, then the mental relationship is customary. Blindness: it is set for the absence of sight from something that is apt to be sighted, and sight is rationally necessary for it; for the mind judges that it is impossible to comprehend the concept of blindness without comprehending the meaning of sight, due to the impossibility of conceiving the restricted without the restriction. If it is said: If blindness is set for the absence of sight, then sight is part of the meaning signified, so its indication is inclusive not implicative. I say: It is set for the absence attached to sight such that sight is external to it, not both absence and sight together; therefore it is frequently added to sight without a supporting context to be interpreted metaphorically, so understand. And do not be misled that blindness is not expression for absolute non-existence; for the annexed (al-mudaf) when taken as such, the annexation is included in it and the annexed-to is external, and blindness is likewise; for it is non-existence in terms of being annexed to sight not non-existence in itself. The conclusion: the restriction is included in it, and the restriction is external; and if the annexed is taken as such, the annexed-to and the annexation are also external. So the annexation is included in the concept and the annexed-to is external, so sight is external to it. Thus the indication of blindness upon sight is implicative, not inclusive. (Isma'il and 'Abd al-Nabi)
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definition of the three types of verbal conventional indication: mutabaqa (correspondence), tadamun (inclusion), and iltizam (implication). It explains that every conventional word indicates either the entire meaning it was set for (mutabaqa), a part of that meaning (tadamun), or something external to the meaning that is necessarily connected to it (iltizam). The author emphasizes that iltizam requires a mental relationship (luzum dhihni), which can be either rational (aqli) as in the case of 'blindness' necessarily implying sight, or customary (urfi) based on common practice. The passage also clarifies why the author chose 'tamam' (completeness) over 'jami' (totality) to avoid implying composition. Using the example of blindness, the commentary by Isma'il and 'Abd al-Nabi argues that blindness indicates sight by implication (iltizam) rather than inclusion (tadamun) because sight is external to the concept of blindness, which is defined as the absence of sight in something capable of sight.

Definition of the Singular Term and Its Three Divisions
Author defines the singular term and classifies it into verb, noun, and particle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any singular expression as a verb, noun, or particle using the logical criteria of independence and temporal indication.

Core Idea
A singular term's classification hinges on independence of meaning and whether its form indicates time.

In logic, terms are the basic building blocks of propositions. Before we can form judgments or arguments, we must understand how terms are categorized. This lesson presents the foundational division of singular terms (al-mufrad) into verb, noun, and particle, based on two key criteria: independence in meaning and indication of time by morphological form. Mastering this classification is essential for analyzing definitions and syllogisms in the Islamic logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The definiendum is the singular term (al-mufrad). Its definition: a term no part of which is intended to indicate part of its meaning. This separates it from composite terms (murakkab).
The definition is negative: it excludes any term where parts contribute to the whole meaning.
2
Extract the criteria for classification
The passage states that if a singular term is independent in meaning (does not need another word to complete its signification), it is either a verb if its morphological form (hay'a) indicates one of the three times (past, present, future), or a noun if it does not. If it is not independent, it is a particle.
Independence refers to standalone signification; particles like 'fi' require attachment to another word.
3
Test the conditions with examples
Apply the criteria: 'nasara' (نصر) is independent and its form (three fatha vowels) indicates past tense, so it is a verb. 'dar' (دار) is independent but its form does not indicate time, so it is a noun. 'fi' (في) is not independent, so it is a particle.
The condition of declinable material (madda mutasarrifa) for verbs ensures that words like 'hajar' (حجر) are not mistakenly classified as verbs.
4
Distinguish the logical classification from grammatical
The logicians' 'kalima' corresponds to the grammarians' 'fi'l' (verb), but the logicians emphasize meaning and the temporal function of the form, whereas grammarians focus on morphological patterns and syntactic behavior.
This nuance avoids confusion when studying logical texts that use 'kalima' specifically for verbs.
5
Recognize the significance of the 'declinable material' condition
The condition that the root must be mutasarrif (declinable) eliminates counterexamples like 'jasq' and 'hajar', which have verb-like vowel patterns but are not derived from roots that can undergo tense, person, or number changes.
This shows that logical classification is not purely formal; it requires semantic and morphological criteria.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المفرد وأقسامه وإلا فمفرد، وهو إن استقل فمع الدلالة بهيئته على أحد الأزمنة الثلاثة كلمة، نحو: في الدار وخمسة عشر. قوله وإلا فمفرد: أي وإن لم يقصد بجزء منه الدلالة على جزء معناه. قوله: وهو إن استقل: في الدلالة على معناه بأن لا يحتاج فيها إلى ضم ضميمة. قوله: بهيئته بأن يكون بحيث كلما تحققت هيئته التركيبية في مادة موضوعة متصرفة فيها، فهم واحد الأزمنة الثلاثة، مثلا هيئة 'نصر'، وهي المشتملة على ثلاثة حروف مفتوحة متوالية، كلما تحققت فهم الزمان الماضي، لكن بشرط أن يكون تحققها في ضمن مادة موضوعة متصرفة فيها، فلا يرد النقض بنحو جسق وحجر. قوله كلمة في عرف المنطقيين. وبدونها اسم، وإلا فأداة. وأيضاً وفي عرف النحاة فعل.
The singular and its divisions. Otherwise it is singular. And it, if it is independent, and with indication by its form of one of the three times, is a word (verb), like 'in the house' and 'fifteen'. His statement: 'Otherwise it is singular': i.e., even if no part of it is intended to indicate part of its meaning. His statement: 'And it, if it is independent': in indicating its meaning, by not needing to attach something else. His statement: 'by its form': such that whenever its compositional form is realized in a subject matter that is declinable, one of the three times is understood, for example the form 'nasara', which consists of three consecutive open letters, whenever realized indicates the past tense, but on condition that its realization is within a subject matter that is declinable, so the counterexample of 'jasq' and 'hajar' does not apply. His statement: 'word (verb)' in the terminology of the logicians. And without it, a noun. Otherwise, a particle. Also, and in the terminology of the grammarians, a verb.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage defines the singular term (al-mufrad) and divides it into three categories: verb (kalima), noun (ism), and particle (ada). The author begins by stating that if a term is not composite (i.e., not a phrase whose parts indicate parts of the meaning), it is singular. Then he explains the criteria: if the singular term is independent in meaning (does not require another word to complete its signification), and if it indicates one of the three times (past, present, future) by its morphological form (hay'a), then it is called a 'kalima' in logic—equivalent to a verb in grammar. An example given is 'nasara' (he helped), whose form with three consecutive fatha vowels consistently indicates past tense. However, this only applies when the form occurs in a subject matter (madda) that is properly declinable (mutasarrif), meaning it can undergo derivation and inflection. This excludes words like 'jasq' and 'hajar' which are not verbs despite having a similar fatha pattern, because they are not declinable. If the singular term is independent but does not indicate time by its form, it is a noun (ism), like 'house' (dar). If it is not independent (needs another word to complete meaning), it is a particle (ada), like 'in' (fi). The author also notes that the logicians' 'kalima' corresponds to the grammarians' 'fi'l' (verb), but with the nuance that the logicians focus on meaning while grammarians focus on form.

Key Terms
مفرد
كلمة
اسم
أداة
هيئة
مادة متصرفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the logicians' definition in this passage, which of the following is a 'kalima' (verb)?
A) 'Zayd' (زيد) because it is independent.
B) 'Nasara' (نصر) because it indicates past tense by its form and is derived from a declinable root.
C) 'Min' (من) because it is a particle.
D) 'Hajar' (حجر) because it has three letters like a verb.

Key Takeaway
A singular term is logically classified by its independence of meaning and whether its form indicates time, with the extra condition that only a declinable root qualifies as a verb.

What Is Mantiq?

The Three Logical Categories of Single Words
Author defines three types of single words and contrasts logical and grammatical terminology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given single word as an ism, kalima, or adat based on the logicians' criteria of independent meaning and indication of time.

Core Idea
A word's logical category depends on its independence of meaning, not on its grammatical form.

In this passage, the commentator explains the first and most fundamental division in logic: the division of single words (mufrad) into three types. This is the building block of all propositional analysis. You will learn the precise definitions logicians use, which sometimes differ from the grammarians' categories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
Look for the three Arabic terms (ism, kalima, adat) in the passage and note the defining criterion for each.
2
Understand the criterion of independence
A word that stands alone in indicating meaning is ism; if it indicates meaning with time, it is kalima; if it lacks independent meaning, it is adat.
3
Note the terminological shift
The logicians' 'kalima' corresponds roughly to the grammarians' 'fi'l', but not perfectly—some grammatical verbs are not logical kalimat.
4
Apply the classification
Given a sample word (like 'rajul' man, 'yaktubu' writes, 'fi' in), determine its logical category using the criteria.

المفرد وأقسامه
وبدونها اسم، وإلا فأداة وأيضاً
وفي عرف النحاة فعل. قوله وإلا فأداة أي وإن لم يستقل في الدلالة فأداة في عرف المنطقيين، وحرف في عرف النحاة. قوله وأيضاً مفعول مطلق لفعل محذوف: أي آض أيضا، أي رجع رجوعا، وفيه إشارة إلى أن هذه القسمة أيضاً لمطلق المفرد لا للاسم، وفيه بحث؛ لأنه يقتضي أن يكون الحرف والفعل إذا كانا متحدي المعنى داخلين في العلم والمتواطي والمشكك مع أنهم لا يسمونهما بهذه الأسامي، بل قد حقق في موضعه أن معناهما من فعل يعني ما يقوله المنطقيون إنه كلمة هو بعينه ما يقوله النحاة إنه فعل وهذا حق. ثم اعلم أنه ليس كل ما يطلق عليه لفظ الفعل عند النحاة كلمة عند المنطقيين؛ فإن صيغ المضارع المخاطب والمتكلم أفعال عند النحاة وليست بكلمات عند المنطقيين؛ لأن نظرهم إلى المعاني، ومعاني هذه الصيغ تحتمل الصدق والكذب وهو ظاهر، والمحتمل لهما هي المركبات التامة فعلم أن هذه الصيغ مركبات تامة فكيف تكون كلمات؛ فإن الكلمة أقسام المفرد. (إسماعيل فأداة: اعلم ليس كل أداة عند المنطقيين حرفا عند النحاة؛ فإن الكلمات الوجودية أفعال عندهم وليست بكلمات عند المنطقيين بل أداة وجه الفرق أن نظر النحاة إلى ألفاظها. فلما نظروا إلى ألفاظها وجدوها مشاركة لجميع الأفعال في إجراء الأحكام اللفظية حكموا بأنها أفعال ونظر المنطقيين إلى المعاني، فلما نظروا إليها وجدوها مشاركة لجميع الأدوات في عدم الاستقلال وحكموا بأنها أدوات فإن قلت: إنها إذا كانت أدوات عند المنطقيين فيم سميت كلمات وجودية في عرفهم؟ قلت لمشابهتها الكلمات في التصرف والدلالة على الزمان، فهي أدوات بالحقيقة وإن أطلق عليها الكلمات مجازا. (إسماعيل) وفيه بحث قد قيل: إن هذا التقسيم راجع إلى المفرد المطلق؛ لأن الفعل أيضا يكون متواطيا ومشككا ومشتركا ومنقولا وحقيقة ومجازاً؛ فإن "ذهب" مثلا متواط و"وجد" مشكك و "ضرب" مشترك و"صلى" منقول "ونطق الإنسان" حقيقة و نطق الحال مجاز، وكذا حال الحرف؛ فإن "من" مثلا مشترك بين الإبتداء والتبعيض و"في" حقيقة إذا استعملت بمعنى الظرفية ومحاز إذا استعملت بمعنى "على" فتأمل. قد حقق ولما كان هذا الكلام دالا على إمكان هذه التسمية وعدم وقوعها قال: بل قد حقق إلخ، يعني القسمة ممتنعة؛ لأن كلا من المتواطي والمشكك لا يكون إلا كليا، فلما لم يتصف معناهما بالكلية لا يتصور المتواطي والمشكك ههنا والعلم لا يكون إلا جزئيا، فلما لم يتصف معناهما بالجزئية كيف يتصور العلم فيه؛ فإن الجزئي أعم من العلم ونفي العام يستلزم نفي الخاص، كما لا يخفى. (عبد) أن هذه
The single [word] and its divisions. Without it [meaning?] it is a noun, otherwise it is a particle, and also. According to the grammarians, it is a verb. His statement: 'otherwise it is a particle' means if it does not stand alone in indication, then in the terminology of the logicians it is a particle, and in the terminology of the grammarians it is a letter [harf]. His statement: 'and also' is an absolute object of a deleted verb: meaning 'it returned also', i.e., 'it returned a return', and it contains an indication that this division is for the absolute single word, not for the noun, and there is investigation because it requires that the letter and verb, if they have unified meaning, enter into proper noun, univocal, and ambiguous, while they do not call them by these names. Rather, it has been verified in its place that their meaning from 'verb' means what the logicians call 'kalima' is exactly what the grammarians call 'fi'l', and this is true. Then know that not everything that is called 'fi'l' by the grammarians is 'kalima' by the logicians; for the forms of the present tense addressed [to you] and the speaker are verbs according to grammarians but are not 'kalima' according to logicians because their consideration is of meanings, and the meanings of these forms admit truth and falsehood, which is apparent, and what admits them are complete compounds, so it is known that these forms are complete compounds, so how can they be 'kalima'? For 'kalima' is a part of the single word. (Isma'il: And a particle: Know that not every particle according to the logicians is a letter according to the grammarians; for the existential words are verbs according to them and are not 'kalima' according to the logicians but a particle. The difference is that the grammarians' consideration is of their expressions. When they looked at their expressions, they found them sharing with all verbs in the application of verbal judgments, so they ruled that they are verbs. The logicians' consideration is of meanings; when they looked at them, they found them sharing with all particles in lacking independence, so they ruled that they are particles. If you say: If they are particles according to the logicians, why are they called existential 'kalima' in their terminology? I say: Because of their similarity to 'kalima' in declension and indication of time. So they are particles in reality, even if they are called 'kalima' metaphorically. (Isma'il) And in it there is investigation: It has been said that this division refers to the absolute single word because the verb also can be univocal, ambiguous, equivocal, transferred, literal, and metaphorical; for example, 'dhahaba' is univocal, 'wajada' is ambiguous, 'daraba' is equivocal, 'salla' is transferred, 'nataqa al-insanu' is literal, and 'nataqa al-halu' is metaphorical. Similarly for the particle; for example, 'min' is equivocal between beginning and partitive, and 'fi' is literal when used to mean containment and metaphorical when used to mean 'ala'. So ponder. It has been verified. And since this statement indicates the possibility of this naming and its non-occurrence, he said: 'Rather it has been verified etc.', meaning the division is impossible because both the univocal and ambiguous are only universal, and since their meanings are not characterized by universality, the univocal and ambiguous are not conceivable here, and the proper noun is only particular, so how can the proper noun be conceived? For the particular is more general than the proper noun, and the negation of the general necessitates the negation of the specific, as is not hidden. (Abd) that this...
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the first division of single words (mufrad) into three categories: noun (ism), verb (fi'l), and particle (adat). The commentator clarifies the matn's statement 'without it is a noun, otherwise a particle' and explains that the logicians' classification is based on whether the word stands alone in indicating meaning. If it does not stand alone, it is a 'kalima' in logical terminology, which corresponds to the grammarians' 'fi'l' (verb). The commentator then addresses a potential confusion: not every verb in grammar is a 'kalima' in logic; for example, present tense forms addressed to 'you' or the speaker are verbs in grammar but are considered complete compound sentences in logic because they convey truth-valued propositions. The commentator also discusses the existence of 'existential words' (kalimat wujūdiyya) like 'exists' (mawjūd) which grammarians call verbs but logicians treat as particles because they lack independent meaning. The passage then presents a critique: the division might apply to the absolute single word (al-mufrad al-mutlaq) rather than solely to nouns, because verbs and particles can also be classified as univocal, ambiguous, etc. The commentator argues that this is not possible because univocal and ambiguous terms must be universal, while proper nouns are particular; since verbs and particles are not universal or particular in the same way, they cannot enter into that classification. The passage ends with a gloss by 'Abd' that is incomplete.

اسم
كلمة
أداة

Key Takeaway
The first division of mufrad in logic is based on independence of meaning and indication of time, not on grammatical parts of speech.

When a Verb is Not a Word: Present Tense Forms
Commentator explains why some grammatical verbs are not logical kalimat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which grammatical verbs (fi'l) are considered logical compounds (tam) rather than single words (kalima).

Core Idea
Present tense verbs for 'you' and 'I' are full sentences, not single words, in logical analysis.

The commentator raises an important nuance: not every verb in grammar is a single word in logic. Specifically, present tense forms used for the addressee or speaker are considered complete compound statements because they convey a proposition that can be true or false. This distinction is crucial for correctly analyzing the components of syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Find where the commentator says that some grammatical verbs are not kalima.
2
Understand the reason
The logicians consider meaning; present tense second/first person forms express a full sentence (you kill, I kill) which can be true or false.
3
Contrast with third person
Third person present tense (yadribu) is considered kalima because it does not include a subject pronoun in the same way? The passage doesn't specify, but it says 'addressed and speaker forms' are not kalima, implying third person is kalima.
4
Apply the distinction
Given a list of Arabic verbs, determine which are kalima and which are complete compounds according to logicians.

Can Verbs and Particles Be Univocal or Ambiguous?
Commentator critiques the extension of mufrad classification to verbs and particles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the claim that the logical division of mufrad into proper noun, univocal, ambiguous, etc., applies only to nouns.

Core Idea
Only nouns can be universal or particular enough to fit the categories of univocal and ambiguous.

The passage presents a critical discussion: some scholars think that the division of mufrad into categories like proper noun, univocal, ambiguous, etc., applies to all single words, including verbs and particles. The commentator argues that this is impossible because univocal and ambiguous terms must be universal, and proper nouns must be particular. Verbs and particles lack these features. This lesson examines the argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
State the claim
The division into proper noun, univocal, ambiguous, etc., is said to be for absolute mufrad.
2
Find the objection
The commentator says that univocal and ambiguous are only for universals, and proper noun only for particulars.
3
Understand the reasoning
Verbs and particles are not universal or particular in the relevant sense; their meanings are not predicable of many (universal) or specific individuals (particular).
4
Evaluate the examples
The passage gives examples like 'dhahaba' as univocal and 'min' as equivocal, but the commentator argues that these are not truly applicable. Discuss how this tension is resolved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المفرد وأقسامه
وبدونها اسم، وإلا فأداة وأيضاً
وفي عرف النحاة فعل. قوله وإلا فأداة أي وإن لم يستقل في الدلالة فأداة في عرف المنطقيين، وحرف في عرف النحاة. قوله وأيضاً مفعول مطلق لفعل محذوف: أي آض أيضا، أي رجع رجوعا، وفيه إشارة إلى أن هذه القسمة أيضاً لمطلق المفرد لا للاسم، وفيه بحث؛ لأنه يقتضي أن يكون الحرف والفعل إذا كانا متحدي المعنى داخلين في العلم والمتواطي والمشكك مع أنهم لا يسمونهما بهذه الأسامي، بل قد حقق في موضعه أن معناهما من فعل يعني ما يقوله المنطقيون إنه كلمة هو بعينه ما يقوله النحاة إنه فعل وهذا حق. ثم اعلم أنه ليس كل ما يطلق عليه لفظ الفعل عند النحاة كلمة عند المنطقيين؛ فإن صيغ المضارع المخاطب والمتكلم أفعال عند النحاة وليست بكلمات عند المنطقيين؛ لأن نظرهم إلى المعاني، ومعاني هذه الصيغ تحتمل الصدق والكذب وهو ظاهر، والمحتمل لهما هي المركبات التامة فعلم أن هذه الصيغ مركبات تامة فكيف تكون كلمات؛ فإن الكلمة أقسام المفرد. (إسماعيل فأداة: اعلم ليس كل أداة عند المنطقيين حرفا عند النحاة؛ فإن الكلمات الوجودية أفعال عندهم وليست بكلمات عند المنطقيين بل أداة وجه الفرق أن نظر النحاة إلى ألفاظها. فلما نظروا إلى ألفاظها وجدوها مشاركة لجميع الأفعال في إجراء الأحكام اللفظية حكموا بأنها أفعال ونظر المنطقيين إلى المعاني، فلما نظروا إليها وجدوها مشاركة لجميع الأدوات في عدم الاستقلال وحكموا بأنها أدوات فإن قلت: إنها إذا كانت أدوات عند المنطقيين فيم سميت كلمات وجودية في عرفهم؟ قلت لمشابهتها الكلمات في التصرف والدلالة على الزمان، فهي أدوات بالحقيقة وإن أطلق عليها الكلمات مجازا. (إسماعيل) وفيه بحث قد قيل: إن هذا التقسيم راجع إلى المفرد المطلق؛ لأن الفعل أيضا يكون متواطيا ومشككا ومشتركا ومنقولا وحقيقة ومجازاً؛ فإن "ذهب" مثلا متواط و"وجد" مشكك و "ضرب" مشترك و"صلى" منقول "ونطق الإنسان" حقيقة و نطق الحال مجاز، وكذا حال الحرف؛ فإن "من" مثلا مشترك بين الإبتداء والتبعيض و"في" حقيقة إذا استعملت بمعنى الظرفية ومحاز إذا استعملت بمعنى "على" فتأمل. قد حقق ولما كان هذا الكلام دالا على إمكان هذه التسمية وعدم وقوعها قال: بل قد حقق إلخ، يعني القسمة ممتنعة؛ لأن كلا من المتواطي والمشكك لا يكون إلا كليا، فلما لم يتصف معناهما بالكلية لا يتصور المتواطي والمشكك ههنا والعلم لا يكون إلا جزئيا، فلما لم يتصف معناهما بالجزئية كيف يتصور العلم فيه؛ فإن الجزئي أعم من العلم ونفي العام يستلزم نفي الخاص، كما لا يخفى. (عبد) أن هذه
The single [word] and its divisions. Without it [meaning?] it is a noun, otherwise it is a particle, and also. According to the grammarians, it is a verb. His statement: 'otherwise it is a particle' means if it does not stand alone in indication, then in the terminology of the logicians it is a particle, and in the terminology of the grammarians it is a letter [harf]. His statement: 'and also' is an absolute object of a deleted verb: meaning 'it returned also', i.e., 'it returned a return', and it contains an indication that this division is for the absolute single word, not for the noun, and there is investigation because it requires that the letter and verb, if they have unified meaning, enter into proper noun, univocal, and ambiguous, while they do not call them by these names. Rather, it has been verified in its place that their meaning from 'verb' means what the logicians call 'kalima' is exactly what the grammarians call 'fi'l', and this is true. Then know that not everything that is called 'fi'l' by the grammarians is 'kalima' by the logicians; for the forms of the present tense addressed [to you] and the speaker are verbs according to grammarians but are not 'kalima' according to logicians because their consideration is of meanings, and the meanings of these forms admit truth and falsehood, which is apparent, and what admits them are complete compounds, so it is known that these forms are complete compounds, so how can they be 'kalima'? For 'kalima' is a part of the single word. (Isma'il: And a particle: Know that not every particle according to the logicians is a letter according to the grammarians; for the existential words are verbs according to them and are not 'kalima' according to the logicians but a particle. The difference is that the grammarians' consideration is of their expressions. When they looked at their expressions, they found them sharing with all verbs in the application of verbal judgments, so they ruled that they are verbs. The logicians' consideration is of meanings; when they looked at them, they found them sharing with all particles in lacking independence, so they ruled that they are particles. If you say: If they are particles according to the logicians, why are they called existential 'kalima' in their terminology? I say: Because of their similarity to 'kalima' in declension and indication of time. So they are particles in reality, even if they are called 'kalima' metaphorically. (Isma'il) And in it there is investigation: It has been said that this division refers to the absolute single word because the verb also can be univocal, ambiguous, equivocal, transferred, literal, and metaphorical; for example, 'dhahaba' is univocal, 'wajada' is ambiguous, 'daraba' is equivocal, 'salla' is transferred, 'nataqa al-insanu' is literal, and 'nataqa al-halu' is metaphorical. Similarly for the particle; for example, 'min' is equivocal between beginning and partitive, and 'fi' is literal when used to mean containment and metaphorical when used to mean 'ala'. So ponder. It has been verified. And since this statement indicates the possibility of this naming and its non-occurrence, he said: 'Rather it has been verified etc.', meaning the division is impossible because both the univocal and ambiguous are only universal, and since their meanings are not characterized by universality, the univocal and ambiguous are not conceivable here, and the proper noun is only particular, so how can the proper noun be conceived? For the particular is more general than the proper noun, and the negation of the general necessitates the negation of the specific, as is not hidden. (Abd) that this...
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the first division of single words (mufrad) into three categories: noun (ism), verb (fi'l), and particle (adat). The commentator clarifies the matn's statement 'without it is a noun, otherwise a particle' and explains that the logicians' classification is based on whether the word stands alone in indicating meaning. If it does not stand alone, it is a 'kalima' in logical terminology, which corresponds to the grammarians' 'fi'l' (verb). The commentator then addresses a potential confusion: not every verb in grammar is a 'kalima' in logic; for example, present tense forms addressed to 'you' or the speaker are verbs in grammar but are considered complete compound sentences in logic because they convey truth-valued propositions. The commentator also discusses the existence of 'existential words' (kalimat wujūdiyya) like 'exists' (mawjūd) which grammarians call verbs but logicians treat as particles because they lack independent meaning. The passage then presents a critique: the division might apply to the absolute single word (al-mufrad al-mutlaq) rather than solely to nouns, because verbs and particles can also be classified as univocal, ambiguous, etc. The commentator argues that this is not possible because univocal and ambiguous terms must be universal, while proper nouns are particular; since verbs and particles are not universal or particular in the same way, they cannot enter into that classification. The passage ends with a gloss by 'Abd' that is incomplete.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator's argument, why can a verb like 'wajada' not be considered ambiguous (mushakkik)?
A) Because verbs are not words
B) Because ambiguous terms must be universal, but verbs are not universal
C) Because 'wajada' is a proper noun
D) Because the division only applies to nouns

Key Takeaway
The categories of proper noun, univocal, ambiguous, etc., in the division of mufrad are limited to nouns due to the requirement of universality or particularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the verb 'daraba' (he hit). The passage says it is equivocal (mushtarak) because it can mean 'hit' or 'strike'? Actually 'daraba' is given as an example of mushtarak (equivocal). Does this contradict the claim that verbs cannot be classified under this division? How does the commentator reconcile this?
?
The passage also says that the particle 'min' is equivocal between beginning and partitive. If the division does not apply to particles, how can 'min' be called equivocal? Discuss.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
8 min
Proper Noun and the Condition of Coinage
Definition and commentary on the proper noun with condition of coinage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the condition of coinage (waḍʿ) to determine whether a term is a proper noun (ʿalam) as defined by the logicians.

Core Idea
A proper noun is defined by coinage for a particular meaning, not by usage.

The second division of singular terms begins with the definition of the proper noun. The author states: if the meaning is unified and particular by coinage, it is a proper noun. But what about words like demonstrative pronouns that seem to refer to particulars? The commentator clarifies that the key is the coinage (waḍʿ), not the usage. This nuance is critical for understanding the scope of proper nouns in logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is the proper noun (ʿalam). Find the phrase in the Arabic that introduces its definition.
The definition is in the first line: 'إن اتحد معناه فمع تشخصه وضعا علم'.
2
Extract the conditions
Two conditions: (1) the meaning is unified (muttaḥid), (2) it is particular by coinage (bi-ḥasab al-waḍʿ). Note the exclusion of terms that are particular only in usage.
The commentator emphasizes 'وضعا أي بحسب الوضع دون الاستعمال'.
3
Test borderline cases
Apply the definition to demonstrative pronouns (asmāʾ al-ishāra) and generic proper nouns (aʿlām jinsiyya). According to the commentary, demonstrative pronouns are not proper nouns because their coinage is for a universal, even though they are used for particulars. Generic proper nouns are not considered real proper nouns by logicians because their meanings are universal; they are only proper nouns in the grammarians' sense.
The text discusses: 'كأسماء الإشارة على رأي المصنف... لا يسمى علماء'.
4
Apply the definition to new examples
Given a term like 'Zayd', check if it meets the conditions. 'Zayd' is coined for a specific individual, so it is a proper noun. Given the term 'this', it fails the coinage condition, so it is not.

التصورات
٤٥
التقسيم الثاني للمفرد
إن تساوت أفراده، ومشكك
وههنا كلام وهو أن المراد بالمعنى في هذا التقسيم إما الموضوع له تحقيقا أو ما استعمل فيه اللفظ سواء كان وضع اللفظ بإزائه تحقيقا أو تأويلا، فعلى الأول لا يصح عد الحقيقة والمجاز من أقسام متكثر المعنى، وعلى الثاني يدخل نحو : أسماء الإشارة على مذهب المصنف في متكثر المعنى، ويخرج عن متحد المعنى، فلا حاجة في إخراجها إلى التقييد بقوله : وضعا . قوله: إن تساوت أفراده: بأن يكون صدق هذا المعنى الكلي على تلك الأفراد على السوية.
وههنا أي في تقسيم المفرد باعتبار اتحاد معناه إلى العلم والمتواطي والمشكك نظر وجرح. والجواب: أن المراد بالمعنى في هذا التقسيم المعنى المستعمل فيه لكنه في القسمة الأولى متحقق في المعنى الحقيقي؛ لأن كون اللفظ متحد المعنى لا يتصور إلا إذا كان المعنى موضوعا له بالوضع الحقيقي؛ إذ لو كان مجازيا لكان معناه كثيرا؛ لامتناع تحقق المعنى المجازي بدون الحقيقي، فاسم الإشارات مثلا داخل في متحد المعنى، ويعرض له التشخص عند استعماله في جزئيات ذلك المعنى لكن لا بالوضع، فلابد من إخراجه من التقييد بقوله: وضعا. ويمكن أن يجاب: أن هذا الاعتراض بأن المراد من المعنى في قوله: إن اتحد معناه الموضوع له حقيقة، فأسماء الإشارات ليست خارجة منه؛ لأن معناها الموضوع له وهو الأمر الكلي واحد بل هو خارج من قوله : وضعا، أو المراد من المعنى من حيث أنه يرجع إليه ضمير "كثر" المعنى المستعمل فيه مطلقا بطريق الاستخدام، فلا يلزم كون الحقيقة والمجاز داخلا في متحد المعنى وخارجا عن متكثر المعنى. وأنت تعلم أنه يخرج حينئذ أسماء الإشارات من قوله: مع تشخصه، وإن كان معناها الموضوع له لكنه أمر كلي لا جزئي. لا يصح فإن المعنى الموضوع له حقيقة في
الحقيقة والمجاز ليس إلا واحد وهو المعنى الحقيقي، وإنما التعدد باعتبار المعنى المستعمل فيه مطلقا. إسماعيل) وعلى الثاني: فإن المعنى الموضوع له لأسماء الإشارات وإن كان واحدا، وهو الأمر الكلي لكن المستعمل فيه لها أيضا متعددة؛ لكونها مستعملة في الجزئيات. (إسماعيل فلا حاجة يعني فحينئذ لا حاجة في إخراج أسماء الإشارات إلى التقييد بقوله: وضعا؛ لكونه خارجا عن قوله: اتحد معناه؛ فإن المعنى المستعمل فيه لها ليس واحدا بل كثيرًا. (إسماعيل الأفراد سواء كانت تلك الأفراد خارجية كالإنسان؛ فإنه يصدق عليها على السوية من غير تفاوت أو ذهنية كالشمس فصدقها عليها على السواء من غير فرق. وإنما سمي هذا القسم بالمتواطي؛ لأنه مشتق من التواطؤ، وهو التوافق وأفراد هذا الكلي متوافقة في صدقه عليها.
The second division of the singular term: If its meaning is unified, then with its particularity by way of coinage it is a proper noun (ʿalam), and without it, it is univocal (mutawāṭiʾ). [Commentary:] 'If it is unified' means if its meaning is one. 'With its particularity' means its individuality. 'By way of coinage' means according to coinage, not usage; because what has a universal denotation originally and is particularized in usage, like demonstrative pronouns according to the author's view, is not called a proper noun. It is objected that it is not characterized by universality and particularity because their meaning is not independent and is not suitable to be predicated, so if it were characterized by universality and particularity, it would be predicated of them; the characterized by an attribute is predicated of that attribute. [Ismaʿil:] 'Tā'ammul' is an indication of the answer to the mentioned investigation: that this division refers to the singular term considering some of its parts, which is the noun, so the divided of this division is the absolute singular term which is a stage unrestricted, not the absolute singular term which is a stage restricted to nothing; for the first has the judgments of individuals ascribed to it because absoluteness is not considered in it, unlike the second, or to what has passed regarding the verb and particle also being univocal and equivocal, etc., so understand. [Ismaʿil:] 'Waḥd' is an indication of repelling what is said: that unification occurs between two things, because it is an expression of the sharing of two things in something, and this is contrary to knowledge. The summary of the repulsion is that what is meant by unification of meaning here is the meaning being characterized by unity, i.e., numerically one, because unification is from unity, and what is meant by meaning is the meaning intended by the utterance and used in it and considered in terms of its concept, so the common proper nouns are not excluded by his saying 'if it is unified', nor are the common univocals and equivocals. [ʿAbd:] 'By way of coinage': Know that there has been disagreement regarding the coinage of demonstrative pronouns. Some said: They are coined for a universal thing with the condition of usage for particulars. Some held that they are coined for multiple particulars with consideration of a universal thing. On the first estimation: the coinage is general and the coined-for is also general. On the second estimation: the coinage is general and the coined-for is particular. The author's choice is the first, and the words of the ancients lean towards it; the second is the choice of the author of al-Sullam and other later scholars. So demonstrative pronouns are excluded from the definition of the proper noun by his saying 'by way of coinage'; for although their meaning is individual according to usage, they are coined for a universal thing, so their coined-for meaning is universal, which is abandoned; usage is only for particulars, so they are of the type of metaphors with abandoned literal meanings. If it is said: this definition is not comprehensive because it excludes generic proper nouns, for they are not coined for particular meanings. The correct answer is that generic proper nouns are not real proper nouns in the terminology of the logicians, for their consideration is of meaning, and the meanings of these things are universal. Yes, they are proper nouns in the terminology of grammarians, for when they consider utterances, they apply verbal judgments to them due to being subject of a sentence, having a state, and being described as definite, etc., and they rule their proper-nounhood. This is how it appears from the words of the investigators. [Ismaʿil:] Then the second division: If its individual members are equal, it is univocal; if not, equivocal. And here there is discussion: that what is meant by meaning in this division is either the true coined-for or that in which the utterance is used, whether the coinage of the utterance for it is true or metaphorical. On the first, it is not correct to count literal and figurative as divisions of multiple meaning; on the second, demonstrative pronouns according to the author's school enter into multiple meaning and exit from unified meaning, so there is no need in excluding them by the restriction 'by way of coinage'. His saying: 'If its individual members are equal' means that the truth of this universal meaning applies to those members equally. And here there is a view and criticism regarding the division of the singular term by unification of its meaning into proper noun, univocal, and equivocal. The answer is that what is meant by meaning in this division is the meaning used, but in the first division it is actualized in the literal meaning; because the utterance being unified in meaning is inconceivable unless the meaning is coined for it by true coinage; for if it were metaphorical, its meaning would be multiple, due to the impossibility of the metaphorical meaning being actualized without the literal. So demonstrative pronouns, for example, are included in unified meaning, and particularity occurs to them when used for particulars of that meaning, but not by coinage, so they must be excluded by the restriction 'by way of coinage'. It is possible to answer that this objection is that what is meant by meaning in his saying 'if its meaning is unified' is the true coined-for, so demonstrative pronouns are not excluded from it because their coined-for meaning, which is the universal thing, is one, rather they are excluded by his saying 'by way of coinage'; or what is meant by meaning from the perspective that the pronoun 'its' in 'its meaning' refers to the meaning used absolutely by way of usage, so it is not necessary that literal and figurative be included in unified meaning and excluded from multiple meaning. And you know that then demonstrative pronouns would be excluded by his saying 'with its particularity', for their coined-for meaning is a universal thing, not particular. It is not correct, for the true coined-for meaning in literal and figurative is only one, the literal meaning; multiplicity is only by consideration of the meaning used absolutely. [Ismaʿil:] And on the second: the coined-for meaning of demonstrative pronouns, although it is one (the universal thing), their used meaning is also multiple because they are used for particulars. [Ismaʿil:] So then there is no need in excluding demonstrative pronouns by the restriction 'by way of coinage', because they are excluded by his saying 'if its meaning is unified'; for their used meaning is not one but many. [Ismaʿil:] The individuals, whether those individuals are external like 'human', for it applies to them equally without difference, or mental like 'sun', its application to them is equal without difference. And this division is called univocal because it is derived from 'mutawāṭa'a', which is agreement, and the individuals of this universal agree in its application to them.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the second division of singular terms (al-mufrad) based on whether their meaning is unified (muttaḥid) or multiple (mutakaththir). The author first states the division: if the meaning is unified, then if it is particular by coinage (waḍʿ), it is a proper noun (ʿalam); if without particularity, it is univocal (mutawāṭiʾ). Then a discussion ensues about demonstrative pronouns (asmāʾ al-ishāra) and the condition 'by way of coinage'. The commentator explains that the division applies only to nouns, not verbs or particles, and clarifies that the unification of meaning means numerical unity of the concept intended by the utterance. He addresses the objection that unification requires two things, explaining that here it means the meaning is one in number. The discussion then delves into the coinage of demonstrative pronouns, noting two views: one that they are coined for a universal but used for particulars (the author's view), and another that they are coined for multiple particulars with a universal consideration. The commentator argues that on either view, demonstrative pronouns are not proper nouns because their coined-for meaning is universal; they are metaphors. The passage also considers generic proper nouns (aʿlām jinsiyya), distinguishing logicians' treatment from grammarians'. Finally, the definition of univocal (mutawāṭiʾ) is given as a universal whose members are equal in its application, with examples like 'human' for external individuals and 'sun' for mental individuals.

A) Because they are not coined for particular meanings.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between coinage and usage is fundamental to the definition of proper nouns in logic; a term's particularity must be by coinage, not by accidental usage.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the term 'Qur'an'. Is it a proper noun in the logician's sense? Explain using the condition of coinage. (Answer: Yes, it is coined for the specific revealed book, not for any recitation.)
?
Explain why the term 'kitaab' (book) is not a proper noun. (Answer: It is coined for a universal concept; its coinage is not for a particular book.)

Univocal and Equivocal Terms
Definitions of univocal and equivocal with commentary on the interpretation of 'meaning'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish univocal (mutawāṭiʾ) from equivocal (mushakkik) terms by checking the equality of individuals in the application of the universal meaning.

Core Idea
Univocal terms apply equally to all individuals; equivocal terms apply with priority and posteriority.

After defining the proper noun, the author proceeds to the remaining unified-meaning terms. If the meaning is unified but not particular by coinage, it is either univocal or equivocal, depending on whether its individuals are equal or not in the truth of the universal. The commentator elaborates on the definition and addresses a key issue: what exactly is meant by 'meaning' in this division — the coined-for meaning or the used meaning? Let's explore.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two divisions
After the proper noun, the author states: if individuals are equal, it is univocal; if unequal, equivocal. Find this in the Arabic: 'إن تساوت أفراده، ومشكك'.
The phrase 'إن تساوت أفراده' applies to the univocal, and the opposite for equivocal.
2
Extract the definitions
Univocal: a universal term whose individuals are equal in the truth of the universal. Equivocal: a universal term whose individuals are not equal; some have priority (e.g., substance in existence) and others posteriority (accident).
The commentator gives examples: 'human' (univocal), 'existence' (equivocal).
3
Understand the commentary on 'meaning'
The commentator notes a discussion: what is meant by 'meaning' in this division? If it is the true coined-for meaning, then literal and figurative would not be considered multiple meanings. If it is the used meaning, then demonstrative pronouns (on the author's view) would be multiple-meaning terms. This affects whether the restriction 'by coinage' is needed.
The text: 'وههنا كلام وهو أن المراد بالمعنى في هذا التقسيم إما الموضوع له تحقيقا أو ما استعمل فيه اللفظ'.
4
Apply to examples
Check if 'sun' is univocal or equivocal. The commentator says 'sun' is univocal in mental individuals (i.e., the concept of sun applies equally to the sun in the sky and the mental image). 'Human' is univocal in external individuals.
