التقسيم الثاني للمفرد
إن تساوت أفراده، ومشكك
وههنا كلام وهو أن المراد بالمعنى في هذا التقسيم إما الموضوع له تحقيقا أو ما استعمل فيه اللفظ سواء كان وضع اللفظ بإزائه تحقيقا أو تأويلا، فعلى الأول لا يصح عد الحقيقة والمجاز من أقسام متكثر المعنى، وعلى الثاني يدخل نحو : أسماء الإشارة على مذهب المصنف في متكثر المعنى، ويخرج عن متحد المعنى، فلا حاجة في إخراجها إلى التقييد بقوله : وضعا . قوله: إن تساوت أفراده: بأن يكون صدق هذا المعنى الكلي على تلك الأفراد على السوية.
وههنا أي في تقسيم المفرد باعتبار اتحاد معناه إلى العلم والمتواطي والمشكك نظر وجرح. والجواب: أن المراد بالمعنى في هذا التقسيم المعنى المستعمل فيه لكنه في القسمة الأولى متحقق في المعنى الحقيقي؛ لأن كون اللفظ متحد المعنى لا يتصور إلا إذا كان المعنى موضوعا له بالوضع الحقيقي؛ إذ لو كان مجازيا لكان معناه كثيرا؛ لامتناع تحقق المعنى المجازي بدون الحقيقي، فاسم الإشارات مثلا داخل في متحد المعنى، ويعرض له التشخص عند استعماله في جزئيات ذلك المعنى لكن لا بالوضع، فلابد من إخراجه من التقييد بقوله: وضعا. ويمكن أن يجاب: أن هذا الاعتراض بأن المراد من المعنى في قوله: إن اتحد معناه الموضوع له حقيقة، فأسماء الإشارات ليست خارجة منه؛ لأن معناها الموضوع له وهو الأمر الكلي واحد بل هو خارج من قوله : وضعا، أو المراد من المعنى من حيث أنه يرجع إليه ضمير "كثر" المعنى المستعمل فيه مطلقا بطريق الاستخدام، فلا يلزم كون الحقيقة والمجاز داخلا في متحد المعنى وخارجا عن متكثر المعنى. وأنت تعلم أنه يخرج حينئذ أسماء الإشارات من قوله: مع تشخصه، وإن كان معناها الموضوع له لكنه أمر كلي لا جزئي. لا يصح فإن المعنى الموضوع له حقيقة في
الحقيقة والمجاز ليس إلا واحد وهو المعنى الحقيقي، وإنما التعدد باعتبار المعنى المستعمل فيه مطلقا. إسماعيل) وعلى الثاني: فإن المعنى الموضوع له لأسماء الإشارات وإن كان واحدا، وهو الأمر الكلي لكن المستعمل فيه لها أيضا متعددة؛ لكونها مستعملة في الجزئيات. (إسماعيل فلا حاجة يعني فحينئذ لا حاجة في إخراج أسماء الإشارات إلى التقييد بقوله: وضعا؛ لكونه خارجا عن قوله: اتحد معناه؛ فإن المعنى المستعمل فيه لها ليس واحدا بل كثيرًا. (إسماعيل الأفراد سواء كانت تلك الأفراد خارجية كالإنسان؛ فإنه يصدق عليها على السوية من غير تفاوت أو ذهنية كالشمس فصدقها عليها على السواء من غير فرق. وإنما سمي هذا القسم بالمتواطي؛ لأنه مشتق من التواطؤ، وهو التوافق وأفراد هذا الكلي متوافقة في صدقه عليها.
The second division of the singular term: If its meaning is unified, then with its particularity by way of coinage it is a proper noun (ʿalam), and without it, it is univocal (mutawāṭiʾ). [Commentary:] 'If it is unified' means if its meaning is one. 'With its particularity' means its individuality. 'By way of coinage' means according to coinage, not usage; because what has a universal denotation originally and is particularized in usage, like demonstrative pronouns according to the author's view, is not called a proper noun. It is objected that it is not characterized by universality and particularity because their meaning is not independent and is not suitable to be predicated, so if it were characterized by universality and particularity, it would be predicated of them; the characterized by an attribute is predicated of that attribute. [Ismaʿil:] 'Tā'ammul' is an indication of the answer to the mentioned investigation: that this division refers to the singular term considering some of its parts, which is the noun, so the divided of this division is the absolute singular term which is a stage unrestricted, not the absolute singular term which is a stage restricted to nothing; for the first has the judgments of individuals ascribed to it because absoluteness is not considered in it, unlike the second, or to what has passed regarding the verb and particle also being univocal and equivocal, etc., so understand. [Ismaʿil:] 'Waḥd' is an indication of repelling what is said: that unification occurs between two things, because it is an expression of the sharing of two things in something, and this is contrary to knowledge. The summary of the repulsion is that what is meant by unification of meaning here is the meaning being characterized by unity, i.e., numerically one, because unification is from unity, and what is meant by meaning is the meaning intended by the utterance and used in it and considered in terms of its concept, so the common proper nouns are not excluded by his saying 'if it is unified', nor are the common univocals and equivocals. [ʿAbd:] 'By way of coinage': Know that there has been disagreement regarding the coinage of demonstrative pronouns. Some said: They are coined for a universal thing with the condition of usage for particulars. Some held that they are coined for multiple particulars with consideration of a universal thing. On the first estimation: the coinage is general and the coined-for is also general. On the second estimation: the coinage is general and the coined-for is particular. The author's choice is the first, and the words of the ancients lean towards it; the second is the choice of the author of al-Sullam and other later scholars. So demonstrative pronouns are excluded from the definition of the proper noun by his saying 'by way of coinage'; for although their meaning is individual according to usage, they are coined for a universal thing, so their coined-for meaning is universal, which is abandoned; usage is only for particulars, so they are of the type of metaphors with abandoned literal meanings. If it is said: this definition is not comprehensive because it excludes generic proper nouns, for they are not coined for particular meanings. The correct answer is that generic proper nouns are not real proper nouns in the terminology of the logicians, for their consideration is of meaning, and the meanings of these things are universal. Yes, they are proper nouns in the terminology of grammarians, for when they consider utterances, they apply verbal judgments to them due to being subject of a sentence, having a state, and being described as definite, etc., and they rule their proper-nounhood. This is how it appears from the words of the investigators. [Ismaʿil:] Then the second division: If its individual members are equal, it is univocal; if not, equivocal. And here there is discussion: that what is meant by meaning in this division is either the true coined-for or that in which the utterance is used, whether the coinage of the utterance for it is true or metaphorical. On the first, it is not correct to count literal and figurative as divisions of multiple meaning; on the second, demonstrative pronouns according to the author's school enter into multiple meaning and exit from unified meaning, so there is no need in excluding them by the restriction 'by way of coinage'. His saying: 'If its individual members are equal' means that the truth of this universal meaning applies to those members equally. And here there is a view and criticism regarding the division of the singular term by unification of its meaning into proper noun, univocal, and equivocal. The answer is that what is meant by meaning in this division is the meaning used, but in the first division it is actualized in the literal meaning; because the utterance being unified in meaning is inconceivable unless the meaning is coined for it by true coinage; for if it were metaphorical, its meaning would be multiple, due to the impossibility of the metaphorical meaning being actualized without the literal. So demonstrative pronouns, for example, are included in unified meaning, and particularity occurs to them when used for particulars of that meaning, but not by coinage, so they must be excluded by the restriction 'by way of coinage'. It is possible to answer that this objection is that what is meant by meaning in his saying 'if its meaning is unified' is the true coined-for, so demonstrative pronouns are not excluded from it because their coined-for meaning, which is the universal thing, is one, rather they are excluded by his saying 'by way of coinage'; or what is meant by meaning from the perspective that the pronoun 'its' in 'its meaning' refers to the meaning used absolutely by way of usage, so it is not necessary that literal and figurative be included in unified meaning and excluded from multiple meaning. And you know that then demonstrative pronouns would be excluded by his saying 'with its particularity', for their coined-for meaning is a universal thing, not particular. It is not correct, for the true coined-for meaning in literal and figurative is only one, the literal meaning; multiplicity is only by consideration of the meaning used absolutely. [Ismaʿil:] And on the second: the coined-for meaning of demonstrative pronouns, although it is one (the universal thing), their used meaning is also multiple because they are used for particulars. [Ismaʿil:] So then there is no need in excluding demonstrative pronouns by the restriction 'by way of coinage', because they are excluded by his saying 'if its meaning is unified'; for their used meaning is not one but many. [Ismaʿil:] The individuals, whether those individuals are external like 'human', for it applies to them equally without difference, or mental like 'sun', its application to them is equal without difference. And this division is called univocal because it is derived from 'mutawāṭa'a', which is agreement, and the individuals of this universal agree in its application to them.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the second division of singular terms (al-mufrad) based on whether their meaning is unified (muttaḥid) or multiple (mutakaththir). The author first states the division: if the meaning is unified, then if it is particular by coinage (waḍʿ), it is a proper noun (ʿalam); if without particularity, it is univocal (mutawāṭiʾ). Then a discussion ensues about demonstrative pronouns (asmāʾ al-ishāra) and the condition 'by way of coinage'. The commentator explains that the division applies only to nouns, not verbs or particles, and clarifies that the unification of meaning means numerical unity of the concept intended by the utterance. He addresses the objection that unification requires two things, explaining that here it means the meaning is one in number. The discussion then delves into the coinage of demonstrative pronouns, noting two views: one that they are coined for a universal but used for particulars (the author's view), and another that they are coined for multiple particulars with a universal consideration. The commentator argues that on either view, demonstrative pronouns are not proper nouns because their coined-for meaning is universal; they are metaphors. The passage also considers generic proper nouns (aʿlām jinsiyya), distinguishing logicians' treatment from grammarians'. Finally, the definition of univocal (mutawāṭiʾ) is given as a universal whose members are equal in its application, with examples like 'human' for external individuals and 'sun' for mental individuals.

What Is Mantiq?

The Four Relations Between Universals
Author defines the four relations between universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the four relations holds between any two given universals by applying the decision procedure from the text.

Core Idea
Four relations govern all pairs of universals: total opposition, equality, absolute subsumption, and partial subsumption.

In Islamic logic, classification begins with the five universals, but the relations between universals are equally foundational. This lesson introduces the four relations that can hold between any two universals, as defined by classical logicians. The author provides a systematic decision tree to determine which relation applies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two universals
Begin with two universal terms. For example, 'human' and 'animal'. The relation depends on their individual members (afrad).
The relation is defined at the level of individuals (afrad), not concepts.
2
Apply the first division: Do they share any individuals?
If no individual of one is an individual of the other, they are in total opposition (tabayun kulli), like 'human' and 'stone'. If they share at least one individual, proceed to the next step.
3
Check if one is completely subsumed under the other
If every individual of one is an individual of the other but not vice versa, it is absolute subsumption (umoom mutlaq). Example: 'human' and 'animal'. If they are mutually co-extensive, it is equality (tasawi). Example: 'rational animal' and 'laughing animal'.
4
If neither is fully subsumed, it is partial subsumption
If each has some individuals not in the other, they are in partial subsumption (umoom min wajh). Example: 'animal' and 'white'.
This covers all possibilities.

Source Text

كل كليين لابد من أن يتحقق بينهما إحدى النسب الأربع التباين الكلي والتساوي والعموم مطلق والعموم من وجه وذلك لأنهما إما أن لا يصدق شيء منهما على شيء من أفراد الآخر، أو يصدق. فعلى الأول فهما متباينان كالإنسان والحجر، وعلى الثاني فإما أن لا يكون بينهما صدق كلي من جانب أصلا أو يكون، فعلى الأول فهما أعم وأخص من وجه، كالحيوان والأبيض، كانا كليين ولم يعتبر النسبة بين الجزئيين ولا بين الجزئي والكلي؛ لأن النسبة بجميع أقسامها الأربعة لا تتصور بين الجزئيين ولا بين الجزئي والكلي. أما الأول فلأن الجزئيين إما أن يكونا متباينين فيكون بينهما التباين فقط سواء أفراد كلي واحد كزيد وعمرو، أو لا، كزيد وهذا الفرس، أو متحدين فيكون بينهما التساوي فقط، من كزيد وهذا الإنسان ولا يتصور كون الجزئي أعم من الجزئي الآخر. وأما الثاني؛ فلأن الكلي إما أن يكون مباينا للجزئي ولا يكون الجزئي فردا لهذا الكلي، فيكون بينهما نسبة التباين كزيد وفرس وإما أن يكون أعم ويكون الجزئي فردا منه فيكون بينهما نسبة العموم والخصوص مطلقا سواء كان الكلي منحصرا في هذا الفرد، كهذه الشمس ومفهومه أو لا كزيد، وإنسان ولا يتصور التساوي والعموم من وجه فالنسبة بأقسامها الأربعة لا يكون إلا بين الكليين، فلذا جعل مقسما وقال: 'الكليان'. إن قيل: منتقض حصر النسب في الأربع بالتباين الجزئي؛ فإنه ليس من إحدى الأربع قيل: إنه ليس أمرا مغايرا عنها كما ستعرف ولا ينتقض الحصر، أو يقال: إن مقصود المصنف حصر أنواع النسب والتبايرن الجزئي جنس يحصل بأحد النوعين، وهو إما التباين الكلي أو العموم من وجه.
Any two universals, it is necessary that one of the four relations be realized between them: total opposition, equality, absolute generality-specification, and generality-specification in one respect. That is because either nothing of either applies to any individual of the other, or something does. In the first case, they are totally opposed, like 'human' and 'stone'. In the second case, either there is no total applicability from either side at all, or there is. In the first of these, they are more general and more specific in one respect, like 'animal' and 'white'. [This only applies] if they are both universals; the relation between two particulars or a universal and a particular was not considered, because the relation in all its four divisions is not conceivable between two particulars nor between a universal and a particular. As for two particulars: they are either mutually exclusive, so there is only opposition between them—whether they are individuals of a single universal like Zayd and 'Amr, or not like Zayd and this horse—or they are identical, so there is only equality between them, like Zayd and this human; it is not conceivable that a particular be more general than another particular. As for a universal and a particular: either the universal is opposed to the particular and the particular is not an individual of that universal, in which case there is a relation of opposition between them, like Zayd and horse; or it is more general and the particular is an individual of it, in which case there is a relation of absolute generality-specification between them—whether the universal is restricted to that individual, like this sun and its concept, or not, like Zayd and human—and equality and generality-specification in one respect are not conceivable. Therefore the relation in its four divisions does not occur except between two universals; hence the author made [the division] and said: 'two universals'. If it is said: the division of relations into four is rebutted by partial opposition, for it is not among the four. It is said: it is not something distinct from them, as you will know later, so the division is not rebutted. Or it is said: the author's intention was to enumerate the types of relations, and partial opposition is a genus that is realized by one of the two types, namely either total opposition or generality-specification in one respect.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage defines the four possible relations that can hold between any two universals. The author proceeds systematically: first listing the four—total opposition (tabayun kulli), equality (tasawi), absolute subsumption (umoom mutlaq), and partial subsumption (umoom min wajh). Then he provides a decision procedure: either the two universals have no common individuals (total opposition) or they do. If they do, then either one is completely subsumed under the other (absolute subsumption) or they overlap partially (partial subsumption). The examples are drawn from everyday categories: 'human' and 'stone' share no individuals; 'animal' and 'white' share some (e.g., a white horse) but each has individuals not in the other (e.g., a black horse for animal, white paper for white). The author then explains why these four relations are only defined between universals, not between particulars or between a universal and a particular. For two particulars, they can only be identical or distinct; they cannot have the 'more general' relation. For a universal and a particular, the universal is either completely separate from the particular or completely above it; equality and partial overlap are impossible. The passage ends by addressing an objection: partial opposition (where two universals are neither fully opposed nor fully overlapping) seems to be a fifth relation. The response clarifies that partial opposition is not a separate type but a combination of total opposition and partial subsumption, so the fourfold division remains exhaustive.

Key Terms
التباين الكلي
التساوي
العموم مطلق
العموم من وجه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Consider two universals: 'Muslim' and 'prayerful'. Which relation holds between them if some Muslims pray and some do not, and some who pray are not Muslim?
A) Total opposition
B) Equality
C) Absolute subsumption
D) Partial subsumption

Key Takeaway
Any two universals must fall into one of the four relations: total opposition, equality, absolute subsumption, or partial subsumption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given two universals from fiqh: 'transaction' (mu`amala) and 'sale' (bay`). Determine which of the four relations holds between them.

Why Only Universals Have These Four Relations
Author explains exclusivity of four relations to universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the four relations cannot hold between particulars or between a universal and a particular.

Core Idea
The four relations are exclusive to universals because only universals can be more general or more specific than another.

The author argues that the four relations are only conceivable between two universals. He supports this by examining two cases: two particulars, and a universal with a particular. This lesson walks through his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Examine two particulars
Two particulars (e.g., Zayd and `Amr) are either distinct or identical. If distinct, they are in total opposition (tabayun). If identical, they are equality (tasawi). They cannot sustain the other two relations because a particular cannot be more general than another particular.
Equality here means they are the same individual, not a relation of universals.
2
Examine a universal and a particular
A universal and a particular: either the particular is an individual of the universal (then it is absolute subsumption – the universal is more general), or it is not (then it is total opposition). Equality and partial subsumption are impossible because the particular is a single individual and cannot overlap partially with a universal.
3
Conclude that the four relations are unique to universals
Since only two universals can exhibit all four possibilities, the author restricts the division to 'two universals' (al-kulliyan).

Source Text

The Objection of Partial Opposition Resolved
Author resolves objection about partial opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to rebut the objection that partial opposition (tabayun juz'i) is a fifth relation violating the fourfold division.

Core Idea
Partial opposition is not a fifth relation but a genus that reduces to total opposition or partial subsumption.

An objection is raised: what about partial opposition, where two universals are neither totally opposed nor fully overlapping? The author responds that partial opposition is not a distinct type but a combination of the existing ones. This lesson unpacks that response.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
The opponent claims that partial opposition (e.g., 'animal' and 'white' are not totally opposed nor fully overlapping) seems to be a fifth relation not covered by the four.
2
Understand the response
The author gives two possible answers: either (1) partial opposition is not something distinct from the four, as it will be explained later that it reduces to total opposition or partial subsumption, or (2) the author's intention was to enumerate the types (anwa`) of relations, and partial opposition is a genus (jins) that falls under those types.
3
Apply the reduction
If two universals are partially opposed, they are either totally opposed in some individuals and partially subsumed in others. For example, 'animal' and 'white' are totally opposed in some individuals (a stone is not animal) and partially subsumed in others (a white horse is both). The key is that partial opposition is not a simple relation but a combination.
The text says 'it is either total opposition or partial subsumption'.

The Four Relations and Partial Contraposition
Author explains the four logical relations and proves the nature of partial contraposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify partial contraposition (al-tabayun al-juz'i) and distinguish it from the other three logical relations between universals using the proofs given in the text.

Core Idea
Partial contraposition is not a fifth relation but the relation between contradictories of terms with absolute generality.

In this passage, the commentator explains the four relations that can hold between two universal terms: absolute generality, generality from a perspective, complete mutual exclusivity, and a fourth called partial contraposition. He then proves that the contradictories of two terms that have absolute generality are related by partial contraposition, not by any of the other three. This lesson will walk you through the definitions and the two key proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four relations
List the four relations mentioned in the passage: absolute generality (al-'umum wa al-khusus mutlaqan), generality from a perspective (al-'umum min wajh), complete mutual exclusivity (al-tabayun al-kulli), and partial contraposition (al-tabayun al-juz'i). Note that partial contraposition is not a fifth independent relation but a term used for the relation between contradictories of terms that have absolute generality.
2
Understand the first proof (al-awwal)
For terms with absolute generality (e.g., animal and human), assume the contradictory of the more general (non-animal) applies to something without the contradictory of the more specific (non-human). Then, by the impossibility of contradictories coexisting, the specific itself (human) must apply. But since animal applies to everything human applies to (absolute generality), this forces human to apply without animal, contradicting the assumption. Thus, the assumption is false: the contradictory of the more general cannot apply without the contradictory of the more specific.
This proof shows that every instance of the contradictory of the more general is also an instance of the contradictory of the more specific.
3
Understand the second proof (al-thani)
After establishing that the contradictory of the more general is a subset of the contradictory of the more specific, if the converse also held (i.e., every instance of the contradictory of the more specific is an instance of the contradictory of the more general), then the two contradictories would be equal. Then their originals (the two terms) would also be equal, contradicting the assumption of absolute generality. Hence, the converse does not hold; the contradictory of the more specific is broader.
This proof shows that the relation is not equality but partial contraposition.
4
Relate partial contraposition to other relations
The passage explains that partial contraposition also occurs within generality from a perspective and complete mutual exclusivity. For example, the contradictories of 'animal and white' (generality from a perspective) also have generality from a perspective, while the contradictories of 'animal and non-human' (generality from a perspective) have complete mutual exclusivity. Both are called partial contraposition because each universal can apply without the other in some instances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التصورات بيان النسب الأربعة وإلا فمن وجه وبين نقيضيهما تباين جزئي أما الأول؛ فلأنه لو صدق نقيض الأعم على شيء بدون نقيض الأخص لصدق عين الأخص، فيصدق عين الأخص بدون عين الأعم، هذا خلف مثلا لو صدق اللاحيوان على شيء بدون اللاإنسان لصدق عليه الإنسان عينه، ويمتنع هناك صدق الحيوان؛ لاستحالة اجتماع النقيضين، فيصدق الإنسان بدون الحيوان. وأما الثاني؛ فلأنه بعد ما ثبت أن كل نقيض الأعم نقيض الأخص لو كان كل نقيض الأخص نقيض الأعم لكان النقيضان متساويين، فيكون نقيضاهما وهما العينان متساويين؛ لما مر وقد كان العينان أعم وأخص مطلقا، هذا خلف. قوله: وإلا فمن وجه أي وإن لم يتصادقا كليا من جانبين أو من جانب واحد قوله تباين جزئي التباين الجزئي: الأول: توضيحه أنه كل ما صدق عليه نقيض الأخص الأعم، فلابد أن يصدق مع عين الأخص، فيصدق حينئذ عين الأخص بدون عين الأعم؛ لاستحالة اجتماع النقيضين، فنقول : كل ما صدق اللاحيوان عليه ولا يصدق عليه اللا إنسان فيصدق عليه الإنسان للزوم ارتفاع النقيضين فيصدق الإنسان عليه بدون الحيوان؛ لفرض صدق اللاحيوان واستحالة اجتماع النقيضين وهذا خلاف المفروض؛ فإنا قد فرضنا بينهما عموما وخصوصا مطلقا، فيصدق الحيوان على كل ما يصدق عليه الإنسان دون العكس فافهم (إسماعيل ولأنه لو لم يصدق كل ما صدق عليه نقيض الأعم صدق عليه نقيض الأخص لصدق نقيضه، وهو ليس كل ما صدق عليه نقيض الأعم صدق عليه نقيض الأخص، وهو يستلزم بعض ما صدق عليه نقيض الأعم صدق عليه نقيض الأخص، وينعكس إلى بعض ما صدق عليه نقيض الأخص صدق عليه نقيض الأعم، فيلزم صدق الأخص بدون الأعم، وهذا محال (عبد الثاني وهو ليس كل ما يصدق نقيض الأخص صدق عليه نقيض الأعم. ما ثبت توضيحه أن نقيض الأخص أعم من نقيض الأعم بمعنى أنه ليس كل ما يصدق عليه نقيض الأخص : يصدق عليه نقيض الأعم؛ فإنه قد ثبت أنه يصدق نقيض الأخص على كل ما يصدق عليه نقيض الأعم، فلو كان نقيض الأعم أيضا يصدق على كل ما يصدق عليه نقيض الأخص لكان بين النقيضين تساو؛ لتصادقهما كليا على هذا التقدير وقد مر أيضًا أن نقيضي المتساويين يكونان متساويين، فيكون نقيضا هذين النقيضين أي العينان متساويين وقد فرض بينهما عموم وخصوص مطلقا . هذا خلف. التصورات كالمتباينين • بيان النسب الأربعة هو صدق كل من الكليين بدون الآخر في الجملة، فإن صدقا أيضا معا كان بينهما عموم من وجه، وإن لم يتصادقا معا أصلا كان بينهما تباين كلي. فالتباين الجزئي يتحقق في ضمن العموم من وجه وفي ضمن التباين الكلي أيضًا، ثم إنّ الأمرين اللذين بينهما عموم من وجه قد يكون بين نقيضيهما العموم من وجه أيضًا، كالحيوان والأبيض، فإن بين نقيضيهما وهما اللاحيوان واللا أبيض أيضًا عموما من وجه، وقد يكون بين نقيضيهما تباين كلي كالحيوان واللاإنسان؛ فإن بينهما عموما من الأعم وجه وبين نقيضيهما وهما اللاحيوان والإنسان مباينة كلية فلهذا قالوا: إن بين نقيضي والأخص من وجه تباينا جزئيا لا العموم من وجه فقط ولا التباين الكلي فقط. قوله: كالمتباينين: فلا يرد في الجملة أي سواء لم يتصادقا أصلا أو لم يتصادقا في بعض وتصادقا في بعض آخر . (عبد) وسواء كانا صادقين معا أيضا، كما يصدق كل منهما بدون الآخر، أو لا يصدقان معا أصلا، فعلى الأول النسبة بينهما عموم وخصوص من وجه، وعلى الثاني التباين الكلي، فالتباين الجزئي عموم وخصوص من وجه أو تباين كلي، أن التباين الجزئي نسبة أخرى سوى النسب المذكورة، فبطل الحصر في الأربع. (إسماعيل) :صدقا أي مع صدق كل منهما بدون الآخر. (عبد) كالحيوان والأبيض فإن النسبة بينهما العموم من وجه وبين نقيضيهما أي اللاحيوان واللا أبيض أيضا عموم من وجه؛ فإنهما يصدقان معا في مادة كما في الحجر الأسود، ويتحقق اللاحيوان بدون اللاأبيض في الحجر الأبيض، ويتحقق اللاأبيض بدون اللاحيوان في الحيوان الأسود، كالغراب. (إسماعيل) عموما من وجه لتصادقهما في الحجر الأسود وتفارقهما في القرطاس والغراب. (عبد) كالحيوان واللا إنسان فإن بينهما عموما من وجه؛ إذ يصدق كل منهما في الفرس ويصدق الحيوان بدون اللا إنسان في زيد ويصدق اللاإنسان بدون الحيوان في الحجر. (إسماعيل) مباينة كلية ضرورة امتناع صدق الخاص بدون العام (عبد) فلهذا : أي لأن بين نقيضي الأمرين اللذين بينهما عموما من وجه قد يكون عموم من وجه وقد يكون تباين كلي اختاروا لفظ التباين الجزئي الشامل لكليهما؛ ينتقض القاعدة في بعض المواد بذكر أحدهما بدون الآخر. (إسماعيل) لئلا كالمتباينين: المقصود تشبيه نقيضي الأعم والأخص من وجه بنقيضي المتباينين، كما هو مقتضى السوق.
Concepts. Explanation of the four relations. Otherwise from a perspective, and between their two contradictories is partial contraposition. As for the first: because if the contradictory of the more general applied to something without the contradictory of the more specific, then the specific itself would apply, so the specific would apply without the general—this is a contradiction. For example, if non-animal applied to something without non-human, then human would apply to it, and animal cannot apply due to impossibility of two contradictories coexisting, so human would apply without animal. As for the second: because after it is established that every contradictory of the more general is a contradictory of the more specific, if every contradictory of the more specific were a contradictory of the more general, then the two contradictories would be equal, so their two contradictories (i.e., the two originals) would be equal—as previously mentioned—while the two originals had absolute generality and specificity, this is a contradiction. His statement: 'Otherwise from a perspective' means if they do not completely overlap from two sides or from one side. His statement: 'partial contraposition' - the explanation of partial contraposition: First: its clarification is that everything to which the contradictory of the more specific general applies must apply together with the specific itself, so then the specific applies without the general due to the impossibility of two contradictories coexisting. So we say: everything to which non-animal applies and non-human does not apply, human applies to it due to the necessity of lifting the two contradictories, so human applies to it without animal due to the assumption of non-animal and the impossibility of two contradictories coexisting. This contradicts the assumption, for we assumed between them absolute generality and specificity, so animal applies to everything to which human applies, not conversely. Understand. (Isma'il) And because if not every thing to which the contradictory of the more general applies had the contradictory of the more specific apply to it, then its contradictory would apply, i.e., it is not the case that every thing to which the contradictory of the more general applies has the contradictory of the more specific apply to it. This entails that some thing to which the contradictory of the more general applies has the contradictory of the more specific apply to it, and conversely some thing to which the contradictory of the more specific applies has the contradictory of the more general apply to it, leading to the specific applying without the general, which is impossible. (Abd) Second: It is not the case that every thing to which the contradictory of the more specific applies has the contradictory of the more general apply to it. Its clarification: that the contradictory of the more specific is more general than the contradictory of the more general, meaning that it is not the case that every thing to which the contradictory of the more specific applies has the contradictory of the more general apply to it. For it has been established that the contradictory of the more specific applies to every thing to which the contradictory of the more general applies. So if the contradictory of the more general also applied to every thing to which the contradictory of the more specific applies, then the two contradictories would be equal due to their complete overlap on this assumption. And it has also passed that the two contradictories of equal things are equal, so the two contradictories of these two contradictories, i.e., the two originals, would be equal, while they were assumed to have absolute generality and specificity. This is a contradiction. Concepts. Like the two mutually exclusive. • Explanation of the four relations: It is the applicability of each of the two universals without the other in some respect. If they also apply together, then between them is generality and specificity from a perspective, and if they never apply together, then between them is complete mutual exclusivity. So partial contraposition occurs within generality from a perspective and also within complete mutual exclusivity. Then, the two things between which there is generality from a perspective may have between their two contradictories also generality from a perspective, like animal and white, for between their two contradictories (non-animal and non-white) is also generality from a perspective. And sometimes there may be between their two contradictories complete mutual exclusivity, like animal and non-human, for between them is generality from the more general perspective, and between their two contradictories (non-animal and human) is complete mutual exclusivity. Therefore they said: between the two contradictories of the more general and the more specific from a perspective is partial contraposition, not only generality from a perspective nor only complete mutual exclusivity. His statement: 'Like the two mutually exclusive' so that it is not objected. 'In some respect' means whether they never apply together or they do not apply together in some instances and apply together in others. (Abd) And whether they also apply together, as each applies without the other, or they never apply together. In the first case, the relation between them is generality and specificity from a perspective; in the second, complete mutual exclusivity. So partial contraposition is either generality from a perspective or complete mutual exclusivity. (Isma'il) 'Apply' means with each applying without the other. (Abd) Like animal and white: the relation between them is generality from a perspective, and between their two contradictories, i.e., non-animal and non-white, is also generality from a perspective. For they apply together in a matter like the black stone, and non-animal occurs without non-white in the white stone, and non-white occurs without non-animal in the black animal like the crow. (Isma'il) Generality from a perspective due to their overlap in the black stone and their separation in paper and crow. (Abd) Like animal and non-human: between them is generality from a perspective, for each applies in horse, and animal applies without non-human in Zayd, and non-human applies without animal in stone. (Isma'il) Complete mutual exclusivity necessarily due to the impossibility of the specific applying without the general. (Abd) Therefore: because between the two contradictories of the two things that have generality from a perspective there may be generality from a perspective or complete mutual exclusivity, they chose the term 'partial contraposition' encompassing both; so that the rule is not violated in some matters by mentioning one without the other. (Isma'il) Lest 'like the two mutually exclusive': the intent is to liken the two contradictories of the more general and the more specific from a perspective to the two contradictories of two mutually exclusive things, as the context requires.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn passage explaining the four logical relations between universals and specifically defining 'partial contraposition' (al-tabayun al-juz'i). The author first states the four relations: absolute generality, generality from a perspective, complete mutual exclusivity, and partial contraposition. Then he proves that the contradictory of the more general is not the same as the contradictory of the more specific; rather, the contradictories of two terms with absolute generality have a partial contraposition relation. The proof uses reduction to contradiction. For the first case (absolute generality), if the contradictory of the more general (e.g., non-animal) applied without the contradictory of the more specific (e.g., non-human), then the specific (human) would apply without the general (animal) — contradicting the assumed absolute generality. For the second case, if the contradictories were equal, then the original terms would be equal, contradicting the assumption. The commentary then explains that partial contraposition occurs both when terms have generality from a perspective and when they have complete mutual exclusivity. It gives examples: 'animal and white' have generality from a perspective, and their contradictories also have generality from a perspective; 'animal and non-human' have generality from a perspective, but their contradictories have complete mutual exclusivity. The term 'partial contraposition' is chosen because it covers both possibilities. The passage concludes by likening the contradictories of terms with generality from a perspective to contradictories of mutually exclusive things, emphasizing that the relation is not simply one of the other three. Throughout, the commentary uses names (Isma'il, Abd) likely indicating different commentators or marginal notes. The logical progression is careful: defining terms, proving properties through contradiction, giving illustrative examples from common categories (animal, human, white, etc.).

Key Terms

النقيض
العموم من وجه
التباين الكلي

A) They have absolute generality.

C) They have complete mutual exclusivity.
D) They have generality from a perspective.

Key Takeaway
The contradictories of terms with absolute generality are related by partial contraposition, which encompasses both generality from a perspective and complete mutual exclusivity.

Refining Proximate and Remote Genus
Critique and refinement of genus division definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish proximate from remote genus by applying the distributive 'all' condition and identify the error in an overly broad definition.

Core Idea
A genus is proximate only if it answers 'what is it?' for each participant individually, not collectively.

In this passage, the author refines the commentator's verbose explanation of how 'ma huwa' questions work, and then sharpens the definition of proximate genus. The key insight is that the word 'all' in the definition must be understood distributively, which prevents a remote genus from being mistakenly classified as proximate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the commentator's error
The passage criticizes the commentator's speech as lengthy without benefit. What concise formulation does the author propose instead? Look for the phrase 'الکلام المختصر الحسن'.
2
Extract the concise definition of 'ma huwa'
The author states: 'ma huwa' is a question about the complete reality. If only by particularity → hadd tam; if only by commonality → genus; if by both → species.
This restructures the commentator's rambling explanation.
3
Understand the proposed definition of proximate genus
The author wishes the text had said: 'If it is an answer about the essence and every participant, then proximate; otherwise remote.' Find where he says 'إن كان جوابا عن الماهية وكل مشارك فقريب'.
This directly defines the two types.
4
Analyze the objection
An objector claims that a remote genus like 'growing body' fits the definition because it answers for both human and plants. Why is this a problem?
It would make the definition of proximate genus too broad.
5
Resolve with distributive 'all'
The answer is that 'all' in the definition means distributive all (al-kull al-ifradi), i.e., each participant individually. 'Growing body' does not answer individually for each participant because it is shared among many species.
This preserves the distinction between proximate and remote.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Know that the commentator's speech from his words: 'Know that 'ma huwa' is a question etc.' is a lengthy digression without benefit, not free from clumsiness. The good concise statement is: 'ma huwa' is a question about the complete reality. If this question is only with respect to particularity, the answer is the complete definition. If it is only with respect to commonality, the answer is the genus. If it is with respect to both, the answer is the species. (Ismail) Then he began to divide the genus into proximate and remote, and it is not hidden that if the author had said: 'If it is an answer about the essence and every participant, then it is proximate like animal; otherwise it is remote like body' it would be clearer and more concise. (Abd) And regarding each one, an answer to what was raised against the author: that the definition of proximate genus applies to the remote, like the growing body (al-nami) applies to it that it is the answer to the question about the essence like human and about some participants in it like plants with 'what is it?' So the answer to the question about that essence and about all participants in it with 'what is it?' is that the answer to the question about human and all participants in the growing body is the growing body itself, and it is the answer about it and about plants, and likewise in all remote genera. And the summary of the answer is that the 'all' occurring in the author's statement 'about all' is the distributive 'all' not the collective 'all', and the commentator's saying 'and each one of the essences' indicates that. Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is critical: it critiques the commentator's (sharh) verbose explanation of the question 'ma huwa' and proposes a more concise formulation. The author (or a marginal note) states that the commentator's speech is lengthy without benefit and not free from weakness. The preferred concise statement is that 'ma huwa' asks about the complete reality; if the question is by particularity, answer is hadd tam; if by commonality, answer is jins; if by both, answer is naw'. Then the passage discusses the division of genus into proximate (qareeb) and remote (ba'eed). It criticizes the author's definition of proximate genus, suggesting it would be clearer to say: if it is an answer about the essence and every participant, it is proximate like animal; otherwise remote like body. Then it addresses an objection: the definition of proximate genus applies to remote genus as well (e.g., the growing body applies to human and plants). The answer is that the word 'all' (kull) in the author's statement means distributive all (al-kull al-ifradi), not collective all (al-majmu'i). So the growing body is an answer about the essence but not about each individual participant one by one? The passage ends with 'fajismu al-nami wa'in kana jawaban 'an al-mahiyya' (so the growing body, even if it is an answer about the essence...). This lesson refines the definitions and clarifies a technical distinction between distributive and collective universality.

الجنس البعيد
الكل الإفرادي

A genus is proximate only if it answers 'what is it?' for each individual participant distributively, not collectively, which prevents remote genera from being mistaken as proximate.

Real and Relative Species: Definitions and Ranks of Genera
Defines real and relative species, explains ranks of remote genera, and addresses objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish real species from relative species and calculate the rank of a remote genus using the subtract-one rule.

Core Idea
Real species is the lowest; relative species is an intermediate essence acting as both species and genus.

This passage from Tahzeeb al-Mantiq defines two key types of species in the five universals: real species (naw' haqiqi) and relative species (naw' idafi). It also provides a clear method for determining how remote a genus is from a species. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for accurate definition in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions
Locate the definitions of real and relative species in the Arabic text. Note that real species is not a genus for anything else, while relative species is predicated as a genus for others.
2
Extract genus and differentia for relative species
Relative species is defined as 'the essence that is predicated of it and of others the genus in answer to "what is it".' The genus is 'essence' and the differentia is 'predicated of itself and others as a genus in answer to ma huwa'.
The definition requires the essence to be universal and essential, not particular or accidental.
3
Apply the subtract-one rule
To find the rank of a remote genus, list all the distinct answers to 'what is it' for the participants of that genus, then subtract one. For example, for human the answers are insan, hayawan, jism nami, jism mutlaq, jawhar; subtract one gives ranks.
The rank is the number of steps removed from the species.
4
Address the objection
The objector claims the definition of relative species also applies to individuals (shakhs) like Zayd, because Zayd is also an essence of which genus is predicated. The response requires adding the condition of 'primacy' to exclude lower species and ensure exclusivity.
This refinement shows how definitions are tightened in logical discourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الحقيقي والإضافي وقد يقال على الماهية المقول عليها وعلى غيرها الجنس في جواب 'ما هو ' ويختص باسم الإضافي، كالأول بالحقيقي، وبينهما عموم من وجه؛ لتصادقهما على الإنسان المشاركة لها في ذلك الجنس فالجنس قريب كالحيوان حيث يقع جوابا للسؤال عن الإنسان وعن كل ما يشاركه في الماهية الحيوانية وإن لم يقع جوابا عن الماهية وعن كل ما يشاركها في ذلك الجنس فبعيد، كالجسم حيث يقع جواباً عن السؤال بالإنسان والحجر ولا يقع جواباً عن السؤال بالإنسان والشجر والفرس مثلاً قوله: الماهية المقول عليها وعلى غيرها الجنس: أي الماهية المقول في جواب 'ما' هو '. فلا يكون إلا كليا ذاتيا لما تحته لا جزئيا ولا عرضيا، وجميع المشاركات فيه لكنه ليس جوابا عنها وعن كل واحد من مشاركاتها فيه فرادى فرادى، فإذا سئلنا عن الإنسان والفرس والحمار لا يقع في الجواب الجسم النامي بل الحيوان؛ فإنه تمام المشترك فيهما. إسماعيل) ولا يقع جوابا فإن الجسم المطلق ليس تمام الحقيقة المشتركة بينهما، بل التمام المشترك هو الجسم النامي، وهو جزء منه فهو بعض تمام المشترك، وما هو يطلب تمام المشترك. اعلم أن الإنسان' نوع و'الحيوان' جنس قريب له و الجسم النامي' جنس بعيد بمرتبة و الجسم 'المطلق' جنس بعيد بمرتبتين و'الجوهر' جنس بعيد بثلاث مراتب، وإن شئت أن تضبط مراتب البعد فضابطته أن تنقص من عدد الأجوبة الواقعة عن مشاركاتها في ذلك الجنس واحدا فالباقي هو عدد مراتب البعد، فيكون هناك جوابان إن كان بعيدا بمرتبة واحدة كالجسم النامي بالنسبة إلى الإنسان، وثلاثة أجوبة إن كان بمرتبتين كالجسم، وأربعة أجوبة إن كان بعيدا بثلاث مراتب كالجوهر، وعلى هذا القياس. إسماعيل بزيادة من الشمسية) أي الماهية: يعني أن المراد بالماهية في تعريف النوع الإضافي ليس مطلقا بل ما هو مقول في جواب 'ما هو'، والغرض من هذا دفع ما يرد أن تعريف النوع الإضافي بـ' الماهية المقول إلخ ' ليس بمانع؛ لصدقه على الشخص والصنف؛ فإن الشخص أيضا ماهية يحمل عليها وعلى غيرها الجنس في جواب ما هو؟'؛ فإنه إذا سئل عن زيد وفرس بـ'ما هو؟ يكون الجواب الحيوان وكذا الصنف وهو النوع المقيد بقيد عرضي كالرومي والحبشي؛ فإنه إذا سئل عن الرومي والفرس بـ'ما هو ؟' يكون الجواب الحيوان ولم يتعرض لإخراج الصنف إلى تقييد المقول بالأولية؛ لأنه يخرج النوع السافل بالقياس إلى الأجناس العالية. كما في حواشي جلال العلماء له. (عبد)
Real species and relative species. And it may be said about the essence that is predicated of it and of others the genus in answer to 'what is it', and it is specifically called the relative (idafi), like the first is called real (haqiqi). Between them is generality from one aspect; because they both apply to human. The participant in that genus: the proximate genus like animal, where it occurs as an answer to the question about human and about everything that shares with it in animality. And if it does not occur as an answer about the essence and about everything that participates with it in that genus, then it is remote, like body, where it occurs as an answer to the question about human and stone, but does not occur as an answer to the question about human, tree, and horse, for example. His statement: 'the essence that is predicated of it and of others the genus' means the essence that is predicated in answer to 'what is it'. So it is only a universal essential for what is under it, not a particular nor accidental. And all participants in it, but it is not an answer about them and about each one of its participants individually. So if we ask about human, horse, and donkey, the answer is not the growing body but animal; for it is the complete common between them. (Ismail) And it does not occur as an answer because the absolute body is not the complete common reality between them; rather the complete common is the growing body, and it is a part of it, so it is some of the complete common, and 'what is it' demands the complete common. Know that 'human' is a species, 'animal' is its proximate genus, 'growing body' is a remote genus by one rank, 'absolute body' is a remote genus by two ranks, and 'substance' is a remote genus by three ranks. And if you wish to specify the ranks of remoteness, its rule is that you subtract one from the number of answers occurring about its participants in that genus, and the remainder is the number of ranks of remoteness. So there are two answers if it is remote by one rank, like the growing body relative to human; three answers if by two ranks, like body; four answers if by three ranks, like substance, and so on. (Ismail, with addition from al-Shamsiyya) Meaning the essence: that what is meant by 'essence' in the definition of relative species is not absolute, but rather what is predicated in answer to 'what is it'. And the purpose of this is to repel what is objected: that the definition of relative species as 'the essence predicated etc.' is not exclusive; because it applies to the individual (shakhs) and the type (sinaf). For the individual is also an essence that, along with others, the genus is predicated of it in answer to 'what is it?' If you ask about Zayd and a horse, the answer is 'animal'. Likewise the type, which is the species restricted by an accidental predicate, like the Roman and the Ethiopian. For if you ask about the Roman and a horse, the answer is 'animal'. And he did not address excluding the type by restricting the predicated to primacy, because it excludes the lower species relative to the high genera. As in the glosses of Jalal al-Ulama on it. (Abd)
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is definitional: it defines and distinguishes real species (naw' haqiqi) and relative species (naw' idafi). It begins by stating that relative species is the essence that is predicated of it and of others the genus in answer to 'ma huwa'. Real species is the one that is not relative. They have a general-special relationship (عموم من وجه) because they both apply to human. The passage then explains proximate and remote genera with examples: animal is proximate to human, body is remote. It clarifies that the essence in the definition must be a universal essential, not a particular or accidental. It then discusses the concept of 'all participants' and notes that the answer to a question about multiple different species is the genus that is their complete common reality, not a broader category. It provides the example of human, horse, and donkey: the answer is animal, not growing body. The marginal note (Ismail) explains why absolute body is not the answer. The passage then gives a clear method to determine the rank of remoteness: subtract one from the number of answers about participants in that genus. For human, the answers are: insan (species), hayawan (proximate genus), jism nami (remote 1), etc. The number of answers indicates the rank. The passage concludes by addressing an objection: the definition of relative species might apply to an individual (shakhs) like Zayd, because Zayd is also an essence of which the genus is predicated. The answer is that the definition requires the essence to be predicated in answer to 'ma huwa' as a universal, not as a particular. Also the type (sinaf) like Roman is a species restricted by accidental property, and it is excluded by the condition of 'primacy' (al-awwaliyya) because it excludes lower species relative to higher genera. This is referenced to the glosses of Jalal al-Ulama.

النوع الحقيقي
النوع الإضافي
مراتب البعد

Key Takeaway
The distinction between real and relative species hinges on whether the essence is also a genus for other essences, and the rank of a remote genus is found by subtracting one from the number of answers to 'what is it' for its participants.

?

Verbal Definition: Conceptual or Assertive?
Author defines verbal definition and argues it is conceptual, not assertive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish verbal definition from real definition and explain why verbal definition is categorized as conceptual (tasawwuri) rather than assertive (tasdiqi).

Core Idea
Verbal definition explains meaning without judgment—it is conceptual, not assertive.

This passage settles a key dispute: is a verbal definition a mere conceptual clarification or does it involve a judgment? By examining examples and the commentators' reasoning, you will learn to draw the line between tasawwur and tasdiq in the context of definition. This distinction is crucial for understanding how definitions function in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of verbal definition
Find the author's phrase: 'explaining the meaning of the word' (tafsir madlul al-lafz). It specifies the named among stored meanings, not revealing unknown essence.
This is a tafsir, not a hadd haqiqi.
2
Contrast with real definition
The passage says verbal definition does not involve acquiring the unknown from the known (like real definition). Real definition reveals essence; verbal definition merely clarifies a word.
Real definition gives the quiddity; verbal definition gives the meaning.
3
Analyze the dispute
Two views: some say verbal definition is assertive (tasdiqi) because it includes a judgment about word assignment. The commentator argues it is conceptual (tasawwuri) using the Ghadanfar example: answering 'What is Ghadanfar?' with 'lion' produces only a concept, no judgement.
The judgment about word assignment is linguistic, not logical.
4
Recognize the linguistic judgment
There is a judgment that the word is assigned to that meaning, but that belongs to language, not logic. So the definition remains in the conceptual realm.
This resolves the confusion: the logical act of definition is conceptual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة مبحث التصورات تعريف المعرف كاللفظي، وهو ما يقصد به تفسير مدلول اللفظ. أيضاً كتعريف الحيوان بالضاحك لكن المصنف لم يعتد به؛ لزعمه أنه تعريف بالأخفى وهو غير جائز أصلا. قوله : كاللفظي أي كما أجيز في التعريف اللفظي كونه أعم كقولهم: السعدانة نبت. قوله: تفسير مدلول اللفظ: أي تعيين مسمى اللفظ من بين المعاني المخزونة في الخاطر، فليس فيه تحصيل مجهول من معلوم كما في المعرف الحقيقي. فافهم. ثم اعلم أنهم اختلفوا في أن التعريف اللفظي هل من المطالب التصورية أو التصديقية؟ فذهب إلى كل ذاهب والمفهوم من الكلام الأول؛ فإنه يفهم من عبارته أن المقصود في التعريف اللفظي هو الشرح والإيضاح لا الحكم والتصديق اختار السيد السند الشريف الثاني، والحق الأول؛ فإنا إذا قلنا: الغضنفر موجود فقال المخاطب الذي لا يعلم معناه ما الغضنفر ؟ ففسرناه بالأسد، فحصل للمخاطب تصوير معناه وليس ههنا حكم أصلا ليكون تصديقا نعم فيه حكم بأن هذا اللفظ موضوع لذلك المعنى لكن موضوعية اللفظ من المباحث اللغوية لا المنطقية. (إسماعيل فافهم: إشارة إلى الاختلاف الواقع في أن التعريف اللفظي من المطالب التصورية أو التصديقية، وذهب إلى كل ذاهب واختار السيد السند الشريف الثاني، والحق هو الأول؛ لأنه يقع في جواب "ما" و"ما" يقع في جواب ما يكون تصورا؛ لأنا إذ قلنا: الغضنفر موجود ولم يفهم المخاطب معنى الغضنفر، فيسأل ما الغضنفر؟ ففسرناه الأسد، فليس هناك حكم ليكون تصديقا فيكون تصورا.
Conclusion of the topic of concepts. Definition of the defined: like the verbal definition, which is intended to explain the meaning of the word. Also like the definition of animal by 'laughing', but the author did not rely on it because he claims it is a definition by what is more obscure, which is not permissible at all. His statement: 'like the verbal definition' means just as it has been permitted in the verbal definition to be broader, like their saying: 'al-Sa'dana is a plant.' His statement: 'interpretation of the meaning of the word' means specifying the named of the word from among the meanings stored in the mind, for it does not involve acquiring something unknown from something known as in the real definition. So understand. Then know that they differed whether the verbal definition is among the conceptual demands or the assertive demands? Every proponent went to a view, and what is understood from the first statement is that he understands from his expression that the purpose in the verbal definition is explanation and clarification, not judgment and assent. The second master Sayyid al-Sharif chose the second, and the truth is the first; for if we say: 'Ghadanfar exists', and the addressee who does not know its meaning asks: 'What is Ghadanfar?' and we explain it as 'lion', then the addressee obtains a conceptualization of its meaning, and there is no judgment here to be an assent. Yes, there is a judgment that this word is assigned to that meaning, but the assignment of the word is among linguistic investigations, not logical ones. (Ismail: 'So understand' is an indication of the difference that occurred about whether the verbal definition is among conceptual or assertive demands, and every proponent went to a view, and the second master Sayyid al-Sharif chose the second, and the truth is the first; because it occurs in answer to 'what', and 'what' occurs in answer to what is conceptual; for when we say: 'Ghadanfar exists' and the addressee does not understand the meaning of Ghadanfar, he asks 'what is Ghadanfar?' and we explain it as 'lion', there is no judgment to be an assent, so it is conceptual.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage defines verbal definition (al-ta'rif al-lafzi) and discusses its nature. The author first states that verbal definition aims to explain the meaning of a word. He gives an example of defining animal by 'laughing', but notes that the author (al-Musannif) does not accept it because he considers it a definition by what is more obscure, which is impermissible. The commentator then explains that in verbal definition, it is permissible to use a broader term, as in 'al-Sa'dana is a plant' (where 'plant' is broader). He clarifies that verbal definition assigns the name of the word among stored meanings, so it does not produce knowledge of an unknown from a known as real definition does. The passage then addresses a dispute: some argue that verbal definition is conceptual (tasawwuri), others say it is assertive (tasdiqi). The commentator sides with the first, arguing that when someone asks 'What is Ghadanfar?' and we explain it is lion, no judgment is made, so it is conceptual. He acknowledges that there is a judgment that the word is assigned to that meaning, but that is a linguistic matter, not logical.

Key Terms

تفسير مدلول اللفظ
تصور
تصديق

B) Because the answer 'lion' provides a concept, not a judgment
C) Because the question 'what' is always answered with a judgment
D) Because the addressee already knew the meaning

Key Takeaway
Verbal definition is a conceptual tool (tasawwur), not an assertive one (tasdiq); any judgment about word assignment lies outside logic in the domain of language.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement: 'Al-Akhdam is a type of braying animal.' Is this a verbal definition or a real definition? Use the passage's criteria to decide and explain.
?

The Four Modalities of Necessity in Simple Propositions
Author classifies simple propositions into four types based on the modality of necessity and defines each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the four types of necessary simple propositions (absolute necessary, general conditional, absolute temporal, absolute diffuse) and explain the difference between necessity and perpetuity.

Core Idea
Modal propositions in Islamic logic are classified by how necessity relates to the subject: unconditioned, conditioned by an attribute, by a specific time, or by an indefinite time.

In this passage, the author classifies simple propositions that are inseparable from the subject (necessary) into four categories based on the modality of necessity. These categories depend on whether necessity is unconditional, conditioned by a descriptive attribute, restricted to a specific time, or holds at some indefinite time. The passage also distinguishes between true necessity (impossibility of separation) and mere perpetuity (actual non-separation). Understanding these distinctions is essential for evaluating the strength of claims in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types of necessary propositions
Look at the Arabic text; the author lists them in order: ضرورية مطلقة, مشروطة عامة, وقتية مطلقة, منتشرة مطلقة. Note the condition that defines each.
2
Examine the condition for each type
For each type, identify the condition for necessity. In absolute necessary, it's as long as the subject's essence exists. In general conditional, it's as long as the descriptive attribute applies. In absolute temporal, it's at a specific time. In absolute diffuse, it's at some indefinite time.
3
Analyze the examples given
The text provides examples: 'Every human is an animal necessarily' (essential), 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer' (attribute), 'Every moon is eclipsed necessarily at opposition' (specific time), 'Every human breathes necessarily at some time' (indefinite time). Check if the examples match the conditions.
These examples are from natural philosophy and biology.
4
Distinguish between necessity and perpetuity
The author differentiates between ضرورة (impossibility of separation) and دوام (non-separation without impossibility). The perpetual motion of the celestial sphere is an example of perpetuity without necessity.
Perpetuity is more general than necessity.
5
Apply the classification to a new example
Given a proposition, determine which type it belongs to. For example, 'Every created thing is temporally originated necessarily' is absolute necessary if it's essential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام البسائط
أي ممتنعة الانفكاك عن الموضوع على أحد أربعة أوجه الأول إنها ضرورية مادام ذات الموضوع موجودة نحو كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة ولا شيء من الإنسان بحجر بالضرورة، فتسمى القضية حينئذ ضرورية مطلقه؛ لاشتمالها على الضرورة وعدم تقييد الضرورة بالوصف العنواني أو الوقت. والثاني: إنها ضرورية مادام الوصف العنواني ثابتا لذات الموضوع، نحو: كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع بالضرورة ما دام كاتبا ولا شيء منه بساكن الأصابع بالضرورة ما دام كاتبا، فتسمى حينئذٍ مشروطة عامة ؛ لاشتراط الضرورة بالوصف العنواني أو كون هذه القضية أعم من المشروطة الخاصة كما سيجيء الثالث: إنها ضرورية في وقت معين،
نحو كل قمر منخسف بالضرورة وقت حيلولة الأرض بينه وبين الشمس ولا شيء من القمر بمنخسف بالضرورة وقت التربيع، فتسمى حينئذٍ وقتية مطلقة لتقييد الضرورة بالوقت وعدم تقييد القضية باللادوام. الرابع: إنها ضرورية في وقت من الأوقات، كقولنا: كل إنسان متنفس بالضرورة وقتا ما أو لا شيء منه بمتنفس بالضرورة وقتا ما فتسمى حينئذ منتشرة مطلقة؛ لكون وقت الضرورة فيها منتشرا أي غير معين وعدم تقييد القضية باللادوام. قوله: فدائمة مطلقة: الفرق بين الضرورة والدوام أن الضرورة هي استحالة انفكاك شيء عن شيء، والدوام عدم انفكاكه عنه وإن لم يكن مستحيلا كدوام الحركة للفلك. ثم الدوام أعني عدم انفكاك النسبة الإيجابية أو السلبية عن الموضوع إما ذاتي أو وصفي، فإن كان الحكم في الموجهة بالدوام الذاتي أي بعدم انفكاك النسبة عن الموضوع ما دام ذات الموضوع موجودة.
وبينهما عموم وخصوص مطلقا.
Divisions of simple propositions: i.e., what are inseparable from the subject in one of four ways. First: it is necessary as long as the subject's essence exists, e.g., 'Every human is an animal necessarily' and 'No human is a stone necessarily'; then the proposition is called absolute necessary because it includes necessity and does not restrict necessity by a descriptive attribute or time. Second: it is necessary as long as the descriptive attribute is established for the subject's essence, e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer' and 'No writer is still in his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer'; then it is called general conditional because necessity is conditioned by the descriptive attribute, and because this proposition is more general than the special conditional as will come. Third: it is necessary at a specific time, e.g., 'Every moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun' and 'No moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of quadrature'; then it is called absolute temporal because necessity is restricted to a time and the proposition is not restricted by non-permanence. Fourth: it is necessary at some time, e.g., 'Every human breathes necessarily at some time' or 'No human breathes necessarily at some time'; then it is called absolute diffuse because the time of necessity therein is diffuse, i.e., indefinite, and the proposition is not restricted by non-permanence. His saying: 'so absolute perpetual': the difference between necessity and perpetuity is that necessity is the impossibility of separation of one thing from another, while perpetuity is the non-separation of it from it even if it is not impossible, like the perpetuity of motion for the celestial sphere. Then perpetuity, i.e., the non-separation of the affirmative or negative relation from the subject, is either essential or attributive. If the judgment in the modal proposition is by essential perpetuity, i.e., by the non-separation of the relation from the subject as long as the subject's essence exists. And between them is absolute generality and specificity.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies simple propositions according to the modality of necessity. The author begins by explaining the four types of necessary propositions, each based on how necessity relates to the subject. The first type is 'absolute necessary' (ضرورية مطلقة), where necessity holds as long as the subject's essence exists, without any additional condition. Examples include essential predications like 'Every human is an animal necessarily', where the predicate is inseparable from the subject's essence. The second type is 'general conditional' (مشروطة عامة), where necessity is conditional on the subject being described by a particular attribute, such as 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer'. Here, necessity is tied to the attribute 'writing', not to the subject's essence per se. The third type is 'absolute temporal' (وقتية مطلقة), where necessity holds only at a specific time, exemplified by the eclipse of the moon when the earth is between it and the sun. The fourth type is 'absolute diffuse' (منتشرة مطلقة), where necessity holds at some indefinite time, like 'Every human breathes necessarily at some time'. After listing these four, the author discusses 'absolute perpetual' (دائمة مطلقة), clarifying the distinction between necessity (ضرورة) and perpetuity (دوام). Necessity implies impossibility of separation, while perpetuity is actual non-separation that may or may not be necessary. This distinction is illustrated by the eternal motion of the celestial sphere, which is perpetual but not necessary. The author concludes that perpetuity and necessity have a relationship of absolute generality and specificity, meaning perpetuity is more general. Throughout, the commentary uses examples from natural philosophy and logic to clarify the concepts.

Key Terms
ضرورية مطلقة (daruriyya mutlaqa): A proposition where the predicate is necessarily true or false of the subject as long as the subject's essence exists, without any further condition. Example: 'Every human is an animal necessarily'. [Note: This term is stable across the tradition; it refers to the strongest modality in Aristotelian logic.]
مشروطة عامة (mashruta 'amma): A proposition where the necessity of the predication is conditional upon the subject being described by a specific attribute (al-wasf al-'unwani). Example: 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer'. [Note: No significant shift; this term is consistently used for conditional necessity in modal logic.]
وقتية مطلقة (waqtiyya mutlaqa): A proposition where the necessity is restricted to a specific, determined time. Example: 'Every moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of opposition'. [Note: Stable term in modal logic; sometimes contrasted with 'diffuse' temporal.]
منتشرة مطلقة (muntashira mutlaqa): A proposition where the necessity holds at some indefinite time, not specified. Example: 'Every human breathes necessarily at some time'. [Note: Stable; used for indefinite temporal modality.]
دائمة مطلقة (da'ima mutlaqa): A proposition where the predicate is perpetually true of the subject as long as the subject's essence exists, but not necessarily. Perpetuity is non-separation without impossibility of separation, as in the motion of the celestial sphere. [Note: The distinction between perpetuity and necessity is a key feature of Islamic logic; it may not be present in earlier Greek logic.]
الوصف العنواني (al-wasf al-'unwani): The descriptive attribute by which the subject is denominated, e.g., 'writer' as the attribute in 'every writer moves his fingers'. It can be the essence, part of the essence, or external to the essence. [Note: This term is specific to the discussion of conditional modalities in Islamic logic; it is not commonly used in earlier Greek logic.]
ضرورة (darura): Necessity as impossibility of separation; the strongest modal force. [Note: Stable across the tradition.]
دوام (dawam): Perpetuity, i.e., actual non-separation without entailing impossibility. It is more general than necessity. [Note: This distinction is emphasized in Islamic logic; in earlier traditions, perpetuity was often conflated with necessity.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of proposition has necessity conditional on the subject being described by a particular attribute, such as 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer'?
A) ضرورية مطلقة (Absolute necessary)
B) مشروطة عامة (General conditional)
C) وقتية مطلقة (Absolute temporal)
D) منتشرة مطلقة (Absolute diffuse)

Key Takeaway
The four types of necessary simple propositions are distinguished by the condition under which necessity holds: essence existence, attribute, specific time, or indefinite time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Definition of Contradiction and Its Conditions
Defines contradiction and explains its components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to test whether a given pair of Islamic propositions satisfies the classical definition of contradiction by applying the conditions of mutual truth and falsehood entailment.

Core Idea
Contradiction is a mutual entailment of truth and falsehood between two propositions.

Contradiction (tanāquḍ) is a fundamental concept in Islamic logic used to critique arguments and establish consistency. The definition given in this passage is precise: two propositions are contradictory only if the truth of each necessarily implies the falsehood of the other, and vice versa. This lesson unpacks the definition and its conditions, helping you identify genuine contradictions in theological and juristic discourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two propositions
The definition specifies 'difference of two propositions' (ikhtilāf al-qadiyatayn). Contradiction concerns declarative sentences that can be true or false, not simple terms. Note that the passage restricts the definition to propositions because the discussion focuses on contradictory judgments.
Simple terms like 'human' or 'animal' are not directly contradictory; only when predicated in a proposition.
2
Understand the condition of truth entailment
The condition 'such that each's truth entails the other's falsehood by itself' (yalzamu li-dhātihi ṣidqu kulli kidhba al-ukhrā) means if one is true, the other must be false. This excludes pairs like particular affirmative and particular negative, which can both be true.
Example from passage: 'Some animal is human' and 'Some animal is not human' can both be true, so they are not contradictory.
3
Understand the reverse condition
The reverse condition is that the falsehood of each entails the truth of the other. This excludes universal affirmative and universal negative, which can both be false. Thus, genuine contradiction requires both entailments.
Example: 'Every animal is human' and 'No animal is human' can both be false, so they are not contradictory. The true contradictory pair must differ in quantity.
4
Apply the conditions to Islamic examples
Test pairs of propositions from Islamic sciences. For instance, 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (kullu muskirin ḥarām) and 'No intoxicant is forbidden' (lā shay'a min al-muskiri bi-ḥarām) satisfy both conditions: they cannot be both true nor both false. Hence, they are contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Contradiction and its conditions. Contradiction is the difference of two propositions such that each's truth entails the other's falsehood by itself. From his statement: 'difference of two propositions' is restricted to propositions rather than things, either because contradiction does not occur between simples according to what is said, or because the discourse is about contradiction of propositions. His statement: 'such that it entails etc.' excludes by this restriction the difference that occurs between the particular affirmative and particular negative; • The restriction to propositions etc. is a response to what was said: what is the justification for restricting difference to 'two propositions' and not saying 'difference of two things' to include simples, i.e., concepts also? He answered with two aspects: First: it is a statement of reality, not for exclusion, because contradiction is specific to propositions, so it does not occur in simples according to what is said, i.e., the weak doctrine. Second: contradiction occurs in simples just as it occurs in propositions, so generally there would be no restriction, but since the discourse is about contradiction of propositions because the purpose relates to it and not others, difference was restricted to propositions to define what is intended, not something common to both. The conclusion is that the defined is specific, namely contradiction of propositions, so restriction is necessary. Thus the 'al' in his statement 'al-tanāquḍ' is for the specific instance, i.e., the contradiction that is a ruling of propositions is the difference of two propositions. As for the definition of contradiction of simples, it is known by analogy to contradiction of two propositions after knowing that the contradictory of everything is its negation, and that truth and falsehood in simples are considered in terms of predication. So contradiction in simples is that they differ by affirmation and negation such that from them it necessitates that if one is predicated of something, the other is not predicated of it. So what Abu al-Fath objected does not apply. Regarding 'what is said', the word of criticism is either attached to the negation, so the meaning is that the statement that contradiction is specific to propositions without simples is weak, and it does not occur in simples either, as earlier; or it is attached to the negated, so the meaning is that its occurrence in simples is weak because contradiction is that two things are such that each's truth opposes the other's truth, and that is only conceivable where a relation is considered, so it is not realized in simples. The truth is that it occurs in both propositions and simples, so the dispute is verbal. He who denies its occurrence in simples says: contradiction is the difference of two propositions; he who says it occurs there too says: the contradictory of everything is its negation. So memorize, for it will benefit you: the mediated difference, like our statement 'Zayd is a human' and 'Zayd is not a speaker'; for here it is entailed from the truth of each the falsehood of the other, either because 'Zayd is not a speaker' is equivalent to 'Zayd is not a human', or because 'Zayd is a human' is equivalent to 'Zayd is a speaker'. Shaykh al-Islam because? And the reverse. And it excludes: they may both be true, like 'some animal is human' and 'some animal is not human', so contradiction is not realized between two particulars. His statement: 'and the reverse' means that from the falsehood of each of the two propositions, the truth of the other is entailed. By this restriction, the difference occurring between universal affirmative and universal negative is excluded, for they may both be false, like 'no animal is human' and 'every animal is human', so contradiction is not realized between the two universals either. It is known that if the two propositions are quantified, they must differ in quantity, as the author will explicitly state in its place.
