Commentary
This passage defines contradiction (tanāquḍ) and explains its definition in detail. It begins by stating the matn definition: contradiction is the difference between two propositions such that the truth of each necessarily entails the falsehood of the other. The commentator then justifies why the definition restricts 'difference' to 'two propositions' rather than 'two things'. Two reasons are given: (1) contradiction is inherently about propositions, not simple terms, according to one view; (2) even if it can apply to simples, the discussion here focuses on propositions. The commentator also addresses an objection from Abu al-Fath, and clarifies that the dispute over whether contradiction applies to simples is verbal: one camp defines it as difference of propositions, the other as negation of everything. A useful example: 'Zayd is a human' and 'Zayd is not a speaker' are contradictory mediately because 'not a speaker' entails 'not a human' in this context. The definition also includes 'and the reverse', meaning the falsehood of each entails the truth of the other, which excludes the case of two universals being both false. The passage emphasizes that for quantified propositions, difference in quantity is necessary, as will be elaborated later.

كذب

Definition of Qiyas: Genus and Differentia
Definition of qiyas and clarification of its genus 'qaul'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract the genus and differentia from the definition of qiyas and distinguish the technical meaning of 'qaul' from its linguistic meaning.

Core Idea
Qiyas is a composite statement of propositions that yields certainty, but its genus 'qaul' is technically a composite expression, not just any speech.

This passage introduces the definition of qiyas (syllogism) as the highest form of logical proof. The author breaks it down into its genus ('qaul') and differentia ('composed of propositions'), and explains that 'qaul' has a precise technical sense in logic, distinct from everyday language. Understanding this definition is essential for recognizing how syllogisms are constructed and why they are valued for certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum (what is being defined)
The passage defines 'al-qiyas' (القياس). Locate where the definition begins and note that it is introduced after stating that qiyas is the highest demand in tasdiqat.
2
Extract the genus (jins)
The genus is 'qaul' (قول), which the commentator explains means 'a composite, not absolutely, but the composite spoken or mental'. This excludes non-linguistic composites like medicines.
The technical meaning of 'qaul' is a rigid noun, unlike its linguistic use as an infinitive.
3
Extract the differentia (fasl)
The differentia is 'mu'allaf min qadaya' (composed of propositions). This distinguishes qiyas from other composites like a single proposition or a list of words.
The differentia is implied in the phrase 'qaul mu'allaf min qadaya' even if not spelled out here; the context shows qiyas is a composite of propositions.
4
Distinguish the technical from linguistic meaning of 'qaul'
The commentator clarifies that 'qaul' in logic is a technical term: it is not derived from anything nor does anything derive from it, unlike the Persian linguistic meaning 'gufitan'. This prevents confusion with ordinary speech.
This distinction is crucial to avoid interpreting 'qaul' as any utterance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القياس قول إلخ: لما فرغ عن بيان ما يتوقف عليه الحجة شرع في بيان ماهيتها والحجة على ثلاثة أنواع: القياس والاستقراء والتمثيل، ولما كان المطلب الأعلى والمقصد الأقصى في باب التصديقات القياس؛ لأنه المفيد لليقين بخلاف أخويه؛ لأنهما يفيدان الظن قدمه وشرع في تعريفه وبيان أقسامه. قول: أي مركب لا مطلقا بل المركب الملفوظ أو المعقول وخرج بهذا المعاجين والجوارشات؛ فإنها مركبة لا قول، وهذا المركب الخاص معنى اصطلاحي للقول، ومعناه اللغوي بالفارسية ' گفتن' وهو بالمعنى اللغوي مصدر تشتق منه المشتقات وبالمعنى الاصطلاحي اسم جامد لا يشتق منه شيء ولا عن شيء؛ إذ لا يتعلق به شيء كالجار والمجرور الظرف.
Al-qiyas is a statement, etc.: When he finished explaining what the proof (hujja) depends on, he began explaining its essence. The proof is of three types: syllogism (qiyas), induction (istiqra'), and analogy (tamthil). Since the highest demand and greatest aim in the chapter of assents (tasdiqat) is the syllogism, because it is beneficial for certainty unlike its two counterparts, since they benefit only probability, he presented it and began to define it and explain its divisions. 'Qawl': i.e., a composite, not absolutely, but the composite spoken or mental. By this, medicines and electuaries are excluded, for they are composite but not a 'qaul'. This particular composite is the technical meaning of 'qaul'. Its linguistic meaning in Persian is 'gufitan', which in the linguistic sense is an infinitive from which derivatives are derived, and in the technical sense it is a rigid noun that is not derived from anything nor does anything derive from it, since nothing is attached to it, like a prepositional phrase.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a definition of qiyas (syllogism) and its commentary. The author explains that after covering the prerequisites of proof, he now defines the proof itself, which has three types: qiyas (syllogism), istiqra' (induction), and tamthil (analogy). He prioritizes qiyas because it yields certainty, unlike the other two which only yield probability. The definition of qiyas is 'a statement composed of propositions' (qawl mu'allaf min qadaya). The commentator expands on the word 'qawl', explaining that it means a composite, but specifically a composite that can be spoken or mentally conceived. This excludes non-linguistic composites like medicines. 'Qawl' in its technical sense is a noun that is not derived from anything and from which nothing is derived, unlike its linguistic meaning in Persian (where it is an infinitive). This clarifies that 'qawl' in logic is a term that refers to a specific kind of composite—the propositional composite—and not just any speech.

Resolving the Objection: The Subject as a Separate Part of a Science
Author presents an objection about the subject as a part of sciences and offers four possible answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four possible interpretations of 'subject' when it is counted as a part of a science and explain how the commentator defends each interpretation.

Core Idea
The subject is a separate part due to its central importance, even if it theoretically belongs elsewhere.

A famous objection argues that if the subject is counted as a part of a science, it must fall into one of four categories, each already covered by another part. The commentator provides a resolution: each interpretation can be accepted, and the subject is still counted separately due to its significance. This lesson unpacks the objection and its answer.

Guided Path
1
List the four possibilities
The passage states four things the term 'subject' could mean when counted as a part: the subject itself, its definition, affirmation of its existence, or affirmation of its being a subject.
These correspond to different components of a science: propositions, conceptual principles, assertive principles, and introductory premises.
2
Map each possibility to an existing part
The subject itself is included in the subjects of propositions (المسائل). Its definition falls under conceptual principles (المبادي التصورية). Affirmation of its existence is part of assertive principles (المبادي التصديقية). Affirmation of its being a subject belongs to introductory premises (مقدمات الشروع).
3
Understand the answer for each
The commentator says each of the four interpretations can be chosen as what is intended. For the first, the subject itself, even though it appears in propositions, is counted separately due to the intense focus on it—the whole science aims to know its states.
This justification uses the principle of 'building on the predominant' (بناء على الغالب).
4
Reflect on the principle of predominance
The commentator notes that theoretical propositions are more numerous than evident ones, so the ruling for the majority (need for proof) is extended to all propositions. This makes the subject's separate treatment reasonable.
This principle is common in Islamic sciences, e.g., giving the whole the ruling of the majority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : الموضوعات ههنا إشكال مشهور وهو أن من عد الموضوع من أجزاء العلوم إما أن يريد به نفس الموضوع أو تعريفه أو التصديق بوجوده أو التصديق بموضوعيته، والأول مندرج في موضوعات المسائل التي أجزاء للمسائل، فلا يكون جزءا على حدة. والثاني من المبادي التصورية والثالث من المبادي التصديقية فلا يكون جزءا على حدة أيضاً. والرابع من مقدمات الشروع، فلا يكون جزءا، ويمكن الجواب باختيار كل من الشقوق الأربعة. أما على الأول، فيقال: إن نفس الموضوع وإن اندرج في المسائل لكن لشدة الاعتناء به من حيث أن المقصود من العلم معرفة أحواله، والبحث عنها عد جزءا على حدة هي بناء على الغالب حاصله أن المسائل النظرية أكثر من المسائل البديهية وقد يعطى للأكثر حكم الكل فجميع المسائل وإن لم تكن نظرية محتاجة إلى الدليل لكن لما كان أكثرها محتاجة حكم على جميعها اعتبارا للأغلبية وإعطاء للكل حكم الأكثر إسماعيل) مما يفيد التصورات فيه إشارة إلى أن المراد بالحدود في قول المصنف ما هو الأعم الشامل للحدود والرسوم حقيقية كانت أو اسمية يعني أراد بالحد المعرف مطلقا. الموضوعات موضوع العلم قد يكون أمرا واحدا كالعدد للحساب، وقد يكون أمورا متعددة بحسب مشاركتها في أمر وحداني كموضوع هذا الفن؛ فإنه هو المعلومات التصورية والتصديقية من حيث الاتصال إلى المجهول وهذا هو جهة وحدانية. نفس الموضوع: لا يخفى على من له أدنى مسكة أنه لما تقرر أن موضوع العلم ما يبحث فيه عن أعراضه الذاتية، فلا مجال حينئذ لهذه الاحتمالات الأربعة بل المتيقن حينئذ هو الأمر الأول كما لا يخفى. مقدمات الشروع فلا يكون جزءا، فإن مقدمات الشروع العلم تكون خارجة عن ذلك العلم. إسماعيل) من الرابع: أي التصديق بموضوعيته. من حيث تعليلية وجه لشدة الاعتبار به.
His statement: 'the subjects here' is a famous objection, namely that whoever counts the subject as part of the sciences either intends the subject itself, its definition, affirmation of its existence, or affirmation of its being a subject. The first is included in the subjects of the propositions which are parts of the propositions, so it is not a separate part. The second is among the conceptual principles, and the third is among the assertive principles, so it is not a separate part either. The fourth is among the introductory premises, so it is not a part. And the answer is possible by choosing each of the four branches. As for the first, it is said: the subject itself, even if it is included in the propositions, yet due to the intensity of attention to it because the purpose of the science is knowledge of its states and investigation of them, it is counted as a separate part based on the predominant, the gist being that theoretical propositions are more than evident propositions and the ruling of the whole may be given to the majority, so all propositions, even if not all are theoretical and in need of proof, yet since most of them are in need, a ruling is given to all of them considering prevalence and giving the whole the ruling of the majority. (Ismail) From that which benefits concepts, there is an indication that what is meant by definitions in the author's statement is the more general encompassing definitions and descriptions, whether real or nominal, meaning he intended by 'definition' the definer absolutely. The subjects—the subject of a science may be one thing like number for arithmetic, and may be multiple things according to their participation in a unitive matter like the subject of this art; for it is conceptual and assertive information in terms of connection to the unknown, and this is the aspect of unity. The subject itself: it is not hidden from one who has the least grasp that since it has been established that the subject of a science is that in which its essential accidents are investigated, then there is no room for these four possibilities; rather what is certain then is the first matter, as is not hidden. Introductory premises are not a part, for the introductory premises of a science are external to that science. (Ismail) From the fourth: i.e., affirmation of its being a subject. From the causal aspect, a reason for the intensity of attention to it.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a commentary discussing a famous objection regarding the classification of the subject (الموضوع) as one of the parts of a science. The objection states that if someone counts the subject as a part of the sciences, they could mean one of four things: the subject itself, its definition, affirming its existence, or affirming its being a subject. Each of these possibilities appears to be already included in other parts (propositions, principles, introductory premises), so the subject would not be a separate part. The author then offers an answer: each of these four possibilities can be chosen as the intended meaning, and the subject can still be counted as a separate part due to the special emphasis placed on it. Specifically, for the first possibility (the subject itself), the justification is based on the principle of predominant practice (بناء على الغالب): even though the subject is included in the propositions, it is given separate treatment because the science's main goal is to know the subject's states, and this is analogous to giving the majority rule to the whole. The passage then includes marginal notes elaborating on definitions and the nature of the subject of a science. The commentary explains that the subject may be singular or multiple, and it emphasizes that once it is established that the subject is that whose essential accidents are studied, the four possibilities become irrelevant because the first (the subject itself) is the certain meaning. The notes also clarify that introductory premises are external to the science.

Key Terms
الموضوع
المسائل
المبادي
المقدمات

C) The affirmation of the subject's essence
D) The affirmation of the subject being a subject

Key Takeaway
The subject of a science can be counted as a separate part because of its central importance, even if it belongs to other categories.

The Principle of Predominant Practice in Scientific Classification
Author presents an objection about the subject as a part of sciences and offers four possible answers, with emphasis on the principle of predominant practice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of predominant practice (بناء على الغالب) to justify a structural exception in an Islamic science.

Core Idea
Give the whole the ruling of the majority when the majority dominates.

The commentator justifies counting the subject as a separate part by appealing to the principle of building on the predominant (al-ghalib). Even though not all propositions are theoretical, since most are, the entire science treats the subject as the main focus. This principle is widely used in Islamic legal theory and logic. This lesson explores the reasoning behind this principle and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem the principle solves
The objection claims that the subject itself is included in the propositions, so it cannot be a separate part. The commentator needs a reason to treat it separately despite this overlap.
2
Recognize the justification in the passage
The commentator says the subject is counted separately due to 'shiddat al-iʿtinā' (intensity of attention) because the science's purpose is to know the subject's states, and the majority of propositions are theoretical and require proof concerning the subject.
Theoretical propositions (al-masāʾil al-naẓariyya) are more numerous than evident ones.
3
Understand the principle of predominance
The principle is stated: 'قَدْ يُعْطَى لِلأَكْثَرِ حُكْمُ الْكُلِّ' (the ruling of the whole may be given to the majority). Since most propositions deal with the subject in depth, all propositions are treated as if they do, allowing the subject to be a separate part.
This is a practical methodological principle, not a logical necessity.
4
Apply the principle to another science
In hadith science, the subject is the sayings and actions of the Prophet. Although the subject appears in individual hadith reports, the principle of predominance could justify treating the science of isnad (chain criticism) as a separate part, because most discussions focus on establishing authenticity.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : الموضوعات ههنا إشكال مشهور وهو أن من عد الموضوع من أجزاء العلوم إما أن يريد به نفس الموضوع أو تعريفه أو التصديق بوجوده أو التصديق بموضوعيته، والأول مندرج في موضوعات المسائل التي أجزاء للمسائل، فلا يكون جزءا على حدة. والثاني من المبادي التصورية والثالث من المبادي التصديقية فلا يكون جزءا على حدة أيضاً. والرابع من مقدمات الشروع، فلا يكون جزءا، ويمكن الجواب باختيار كل من الشقوق الأربعة. أما على الأول، فيقال: إن نفس الموضوع وإن اندرج في المسائل لكن لشدة الاعتناء به من حيث أن المقصود من العلم معرفة أحواله، والبحث عنها عد جزءا على حدة هي بناء على الغالب حاصله أن المسائل النظرية أكثر من المسائل البديهية وقد يعطى للأكثر حكم الكل فجميع المسائل وإن لم تكن نظرية محتاجة إلى الدليل لكن لما كان أكثرها محتاجة حكم على جميعها اعتبارا للأغلبية وإعطاء للكل حكم الأكثر إسماعيل) مما يفيد التصورات فيه إشارة إلى أن المراد بالحدود في قول المصنف ما هو الأعم الشامل للحدود والرسوم حقيقية كانت أو اسمية يعني أراد بالحد المعرف مطلقا. الموضوعات موضوع العلم قد يكون أمرا واحدا كالعدد للحساب، وقد يكون أمورا متعددة بحسب مشاركتها في أمر وحداني كموضوع هذا الفن؛ فإنه هو المعلومات التصورية والتصديقية من حيث الاتصال إلى المجهول وهذا هو جهة وحدانية. نفس الموضوع: لا يخفى على من له أدنى مسكة أنه لما تقرر أن موضوع العلم ما يبحث فيه عن أعراضه الذاتية، فلا مجال حينئذ لهذه الاحتمالات الأربعة بل المتيقن حينئذ هو الأمر الأول كما لا يخفى. مقدمات الشروع فلا يكون جزءا، فإن مقدمات الشروع العلم تكون خارجة عن ذلك العلم. إسماعيل) من الرابع: أي التصديق بموضوعيته. من حيث تعليلية وجه لشدة الاعتبار به.
His statement: 'the subjects here' is a famous objection, namely that whoever counts the subject as part of the sciences either intends the subject itself, its definition, affirmation of its existence, or affirmation of its being a subject. The second is among the conceptual principles, and the third is among the assertive principles, so it is not a separate part either. Critical Refinement: What Are the Propositions of a Science?
Author presents an alternative answer that propositions are predicates attributed to subjects, critiques al-Dawani, and defends the subject as a separate part.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify al-Dawani's alternative definition of propositions and evaluate the commentator's response to it.

Core Idea
Defining propositions as mere predicates risks losing the subject—unless every subject relates to the science's subject.

In this passage, the commentator confronts a provocative view from al-Dawani: that the propositions of a science are not composite subject-predicate statements, but rather the predicates themselves. This challenge forces us to re-examine what exactly a 'proposition' is and whether the subject of the science can remain a separate part. The commentator defends the original position by showing that all propositional subjects inevitably trace back to the science's subject, preserving the integrity of the subject as a distinct component.

Guided Path
1
Identify the alternative view
Find the claim that propositions are not the totality of subjects, predicates, and relations, but merely the predicates attributed to subjects. Note the phrase 'أو يقال: إن المسائل ليست هي مجموع الموضوعات والمحمولات والنسب، بل المحمولات المنسوبة إلى الموضوعات'.
2
Understand al-Dawani's argument
Al-Dawani, in his gloss, says propositions are 'the predicates proven by evidence' (المحمولات المثبتة بالدليل). Why might he think this? He is trying to simplify the definition.
Al-Dawani's view is cited from his gloss on Sharh al-Matali'.
3
Examine the first objection
The commentator objects that al-Dawani's view does not conform to the author's original wording: 'the propositions are such-and-such propositions, their subjects are such-and-such, their predicates are such-and-such'. This shows that the original author considered propositions to have both subject and predicate.
4
Analyse the second objection
If propositions are just predicates, then the subjects of propositions that are not the subject of the science would need to be counted as separate parts of the science. This would violate the structure of the science.
5
Follow the rebuttal
The commentator denies the implication by showing that all subjects of propositions are either the subject of the science, a type of it, or an essential accident of it. Thus, no subject is entirely separate. He uses 'man is animal with rational' as an example of type, and 'every moving thing has a natural inclination' as an example of essential accident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو يقال: إن المسائل ليست هي مجموع الموضوعات والمحمولات والنسب، بل المحمولات المنسوبة إلى الموضوعات قال المحقق الدواني في حاشية شرح المطالع المسائل هي المحمولات المثبتة بالدليل، وفيه نظر؛ فإنه لا يلايم ظاهر قول المصنف والمسائل هي قضايا كذا وموضوعاتها كذا ومحمولاتها كذا وأيضاً فلو كانت المسائل نفس المحمولات المنسوبة لوجب عد سائر موضوعات المسائل التي هي وراء موضوع العلم جزءًا على حدة، فتدبر. · أو يقال إلخ حاصله أن الموضوع ليس داخلا في المسائل؛ فإنها ليست مركبة من الموضوعات والمحمولات من حيث أنها منسوبة إلى الموضوعات كما يظهر من كلام المحقق الدواني في حاشية شرح المطالع، وإذا لم تكن مندرجة تحت المسائل فلا مضايقة في كونه جزءًا على حدة سوى المسائل. إسماعيل) ليست هي مجموع إلخ: أي ليست هي قضايا. قال المحقق الدواني: تأييدا لكون المسائل محمولة منسوبة إلى الموضوعات وفيه أي في الجواب بكون المسائل نفس المحمولات نظر فإنه لا يلايم: فيه إيماء إلى أنه يلايم باطنه إما بإرجاع قول المصنف إلى ما قال المحقق بأن القضايا ليست إلا المحمولات المثبتة الموضوع بالدليل أو بإرجاع قول المحقق إلى ما قال المصنف بأن المحمولات المثبتة للموضوع ليست هي إلا القضايا كما هو الظاهر على الخبير الماهر وأيضاً : أي في الجواب المذكور نظر آخر أيضاً. فتدبر إشارة إلى منع الملازمة تقريره: أنا لا نسلم أن المسائل لو كانت نفس المحمولات المنسوبة إلى الموضوع لزم إلخ بسنده أنه ليس شيء من موضوعات المسائل وراء موضوع العلم حتى يلزم عده من أجزاء العلم وراء موضوع العلم؛ فإن موضوعات المسائل اما موضوع العلم أو نوع منه أو عرض ذاتي له، ولا شيء من الموضوعات خال عن موضوع العلم. أما على الأول فظاهر ، وأما إذا كانت نوعا منه أو عرضا ذاتيا لموضوع العلم؛ فإن موضوع العلم موجود هناك؛ لأن نوع الشيء عبارة عن ذلك الشيء مع فصل منوع له، ألا ترى أن الإنسان هو الحيوان مع الناطق. والمركب إما مركب من موضوع العلم وعرض ذاتي له أو من نوعه وعرض ذاتي له، فلا خفاء في وجوده حينئذ. وأما إذا كانت عرضا ذاتيا لموضوع العلم فلأن العرض الذاتي من غير ملاحظة ما هو معروضة لا يقع موضوع المسألة، ألا ترى أن كل متحرك فله ميل طبيعي، فإنه مثاله كما سيأتي، فليس في موضوعات المسائل موضوع وراء موضوع العلم على أي حال، وليس لغيره في المسائل محال.
Or it is said: the propositions are not the totality of subjects, predicates, and relations, but rather the predicates attributed to the subjects. Al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani said in his gloss on Sharh al-Matali': the propositions are the predicates proven by evidence. But there is a consideration in this; for it does not conform to the apparent meaning of the author's statement: 'the propositions are such-and-such propositions, their subjects are such-and-such, and their predicates are such-and-such.' Also, if the propositions were the predicates themselves attributed, then it would be necessary to count the other subjects of the propositions that are beyond the subject of the science as a separate part, so reflect. · 'Or it is said' etc., its gist is that the subject is not included in the propositions; for they are not composed of subjects and predicates in terms of their being attributed to the subjects, as appears from the words of al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani in his gloss on Sharh al-Matali', and if they are not subsumed under the propositions, then there is no harm in its being a separate part besides the propositions. (Ismail) 'They are not the totality' etc.: i.e., they are not propositions. Al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani said: supporting that the propositions are predicates attributed to the subjects. And there is a consideration in it, i.e., in the answer that the propositions are the predicates themselves; for it does not conform: there is an indication that it does conform in its inner meaning, either by referring the author's statement to what al-Dawani said, that propositions are nothing but predicates affirmed of the subject by evidence, or by referring al-Dawani's statement to what the author said, that predicates affirmed of the subject are nothing but propositions, as is apparent to the skilled expert. And also: i.e., there is another consideration in the aforementioned answer. So reflect: an indication of denying the implication. Its exposition: we do not concede that if the propositions were the predicates themselves attributed to the subject, then it would follow, etc., on the basis that there is no subject of the propositions beyond the subject of the science, such that it would necessitate counting it as a part of the science beyond the subject of the science. For the subjects of the propositions are either the subject of the science, or a type of it, or an essential accident of it, and none of the subjects are free of the subject of the science. As for the first, it is apparent. As for if they are a type of it or an essential accident of the subject of the science, then the subject of the science is present there; because a type of a thing is that thing with a differentia that specifies it, do you not see that man is animal with rational? And the compound is either composed of the subject of the science and an essential accident of it, or of its type and an essential accident of it, so there is no concealment of its existence then. And as for if they are an essential accident of the subject of the science, then because the essential accident without consideration of what it inheres in does not occur as the subject of the proposition, do you not see that 'every moving thing has a natural inclination' is an example of that, as will come? So there is no subject of the propositions beyond the subject of the science in any case, and there is no room for other than it in the propositions.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on whether the subject of a science should be counted as a separate part. The author presents an alternative answer: propositions (المسائل) are not composites of subjects, predicates, and relations; rather they are the predicates attributed to the subjects. This is supported by al-Dawani's view in his gloss on Sharh al-Matali', where he states that propositions are the predicates proven by evidence. However, the author finds a problem with this view: it does not conform to the apparent meaning of the original author's statement that 'propositions are such-and-such propositions, their subjects are such-and-such, and their predicates are such-and-such.' Moreover, if propositions were just the predicates, then the subjects of the propositions that are other than the subject of the science would need to be counted as separate parts, which is problematic. The author then elaborates, showing that actually the subjects of the propositions are always either the subject of the science, a type of it, or an essential accident of it. Thus, there is no subject beyond the subject of the science, so the objection falls away. The passage includes a detailed logical analysis using examples like 'man is animal with rational' and 'every moving thing has a natural inclination' to illustrate the relationship between the subject of the science and the subjects of its propositions. The overall aim is to show that the subject of the science can be counted as a separate part without contradiction.

Key Terms
المسائل (al-masāʾil): The propositions of a science; here argued to be the predicates (not subject-predicate composites) that are proven by evidence. [Note: Al-Dawani's definition differs from the standard; author critiques it.]
المحمولات (al-maḥmūlāt): The predicates that are attributed to subjects in propositions; in this view, the propositions themselves are these predicates. [Note: No significant shift.]
العرض الذاتي (al-ʿaraḍ al-dhātī): Essential accident; an attribute that is necessary for the subject and inseparable from it. Used here to show that subjects of propositions always relate to the subject of the science. [Note: Stable in logic and philosophy.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The subject of the science becomes irrelevant
B) The subjects of the propositions would have to be counted as separate parts
C) The predicates become the subjects
D) The science would have no propositions

Key Takeaway
The commentator defends the original view that propositions are composite statements, but the subject of each proposition inevitably relates to the subject of the science, so the subject remains a distinct part.

The Five Pillars of a Demonstrative Science
Author defines the five components of a demonstrative science.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and describe the five components that constitute any demonstrative science in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Every demonstrative science rests on five foundations: its subject, parts, accidents, premises, and problems.

In this passage, the author enumerates the essential building blocks of a demonstrative science. Understanding these five components is crucial for appreciating how Islamic logicians structured their disciplines. Let's unpack each element.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject (mawḍū‘)
The subject is the primary object of study in a science. For example, in logic, the subject is 'intentional beings' (al-wujūd al-dhihnī).
2
Recognize its parts (ajzā’)
If the subject is composite, its parts need to be defined. For instance, the study of ‘illa (cause) in fiqh has parts like types of causes.
Parts here are conceptual, not physical.
3
Note the accidents (a‘rāḍ)
Accidents are the essential properties that are proven for the subject. In logic, examples include 'universality' and 'particularity'.
4
Understand the premises (muqaddimāt)
Premises are the foundational propositions of the science, either self-evident (badīhī) or theoretical (naẓarī).
These are also called al-mabādi’ al-taṣdīqiyya.
5
Grasp the problems (masā’il)
Problems are the conclusions sought, which are propositions that the science aims to demonstrate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجزاؤها وأعراضها ومقدمات بينة، أو مأخوذة تبتني عليها قياسات العلم والمسائل، وهي قضايا قوله :أجزائها أي حدود أجزائها إذا كانت الموضوعات مركبة. قوله وأعراضها أي حدود العوارض المثبتة لتلك الموضوعات قوله ومقدمات بينة: المبادي التصديقية إما مقدمات بينة بنفسها أي بديهية أو مقدمات مأخوذة أي نظرية، فالأولى تسمى
And its parts and its accidents and premises, either self-evident or taken (theoretical), upon which the syllogisms of the science are based, and the problems, which are propositions. His statement: 'its parts' meaning the definitions of its parts if the subjects are composite. His statement: 'its accidents' meaning the definitions of the accidents affirmed for those subjects. His statement: 'premises, either self-evident': the propositional principles are either premises self-evident in themselves, i.e., axiomatic, or taken premises, i.e., theoretical; the former are called...
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is definitional, enumerating the five essential components of any demonstrative science. The list includes: (1) the subject matter itself (mawḍū‘), (2) its parts (ajzā’) if the subject is composite, meaning the definitions of those parts, (3) its accidents (a‘rāḍ), i.e., the definitions of the essential properties affirmed for the subject, (4) the premises (muqaddimāt or mabādi’ taṣdīqiyya), which are either self-evident (badīhī) or theoretical (naẓarī), and (5) the problems (masā’il), which are the propositions sought as conclusions. The commentary explains each term: ‘its parts’ refers to definitions of the parts of the subject when the subject is composite; ‘its accidents’ refers to definitions of the accidents proven for that subject; ‘premises’ are classified into axiomatic (self-evident) and theoretical (taken from evidence). This classification is standard in Islamic logic, structuring a science around its subject, essential properties, foundational principles, and derivative conclusions.

Key Terms
المبادي التصديقية
البديهية
النظرية

A demonstrative science is structured by its subject, the definitions of its parts and accidents, its foundational premises (self-evident or theoretical), and the problems it aims to prove.

Premises in a Science: Self-Evident and Theoretical
Author enumerates the five components of a science; this lesson focuses on the premises component.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between self-evident (badīhī) and theoretical (naẓarī) premises and recognize their roles in a demonstrative science.

Core Idea
Premises are either self-evident truths or theoretical claims that require proof; both are essential for constructing demonstrations.

The author classifies premises into two types: self-evident and theoretical. This distinction is key to understanding how a science builds its proofs. The commentary elaborates on this classification, which is foundational in Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of premises
The passage states that premises are either 'self-evident' (badīhiyya) or 'taken' (ma’khūdha), i.e., theoretical (naẓariyya).
These are also called propositional principles (al-mabādi’ al-taṣdīqiyya).
2
Understand self-evident premises
Self-evident premises are those whose truth is known immediately without any inference. Examples include 'the whole is greater than the part' and 'the law of non-contradiction'.
They are axiomatic and require no proof.
3
Understand theoretical premises
Theoretical premises are those that require demonstration or inference from other premises. They are the doctrines that need to be proven within the science.
They are not immediately obvious and rely on syllogistic reasoning.
4
Recognize their role in a science
Self-evident premises serve as foundational starting points, while theoretical premises are the claims that the science aims to establish through demonstration.

Distinguishing Mabadi' and Muqaddimat in the Sciences
Author explains different definitions of mabadi' and muqaddimat, and mentions the eight heads.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mabadi' and muqaddimat based on their definitions and identify which category the eight heads belong to.

Core Idea
Muqaddimat are always external; mabadi' can be internal or external.

In the Islamic sciences, the terms mabadi' and muqaddimat are often used in prefaces, but they have precise logical meanings. This lesson clarifies their distinction and shows how earlier scholars structured the introductions to their works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms being defined
Find the definitions of mabadi' and muqaddimat in the Arabic. Look for phrases like 'المبادي لما يبدأ به' and 'المقدمات لما يتوقف عليه الشروع'.
2
Extract the key criteria
What makes something a mabadi'? It is what is begun with before the aims of the science. What makes it a muqaddima? It is what the commencement depends upon and is external.
Muqaddimat are specifically external to the science.
3
Compare the two
Notice that muqaddimat are necessarily external, while mabadi' can be internal (like conceptions and assents) or external (like definitions and goals). Thus mabadi' is more general.
The passage explicitly says muqaddimat are external 'لا محالة'.
4
Apply to the eight heads
Determine whether the eight heads (purpose, etc.) are mabadi' or muqaddimat. The author says they are from the mabadi' in the broader sense, because they are external and depend on insight.
The eight heads are external, so they are muqaddimat or mabadi' in the broader sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال : المبادي لما يبدأ به قبل المقصود، والمقدمات لما يتوقف عليه الشروع بوجه البصيرة وفرط الرغبة كتعريف العلم وبيان غايته ،وموضوعه وكان القدماء يذكرون في صدر الكتاب ما يسمونه الرؤوس الثمانية : الأول: الغرض؛ لئلا يكون النظر في طلبه عبثا، فالأستاذ صرح باعتبار الثاني، فعدم اعتبار الأول ،تحكم وههنا زيادة كلام لا يسعها المقام. قوله: وقد يقال : المبادي: إشارة إلى اصطلاح آخر في المبادي سوى ما تقدم وضعه ابن الحاجب في مختصر الأصول؛ حيث أطلق المبادي على ما يبدأ به قبل الشروع في مقاصد العلم، سواء كان داخلا في العلم، فيكون من المبادي المصطلحة السابقة كتصور الموضوع والأعراض الذاتية والتصديقات التي يتألف منها قياسات العلم، أو خارجا يتوقف عليه الشروع ولو على وجه الخبرة، وتسمى مقدمات كمعرفة الحد والغاية والموضوع. والفرق بين المقدمات والمبادي بهذا المعنى مما لا ينبغي أن يشتبه؛ فإن المقدمات خارجة عن العلم لا محالة بخلاف المبادي، فتبصر. قوله: يذكرون أي في صدر كتبهم على أنها من المقدمات أو من المبادي بالمعنى الأعم. قوله: الغرض: هي يذكرون في صدر الكتاب في جميع النسخ الموجودة من القلمية والمطبوعة إلا في نسخة صحيحة قديمة قلمية، فإنه ليس فيها، والظاهر أنها الصحيحة؛ لأن الماتن بصدد تهذيب الكلام وإيضاح قول الشارح أي في صدر كتبهم يكون لغوا على هذا النسخ فافهم. فالأستاذ : جلال العلماء صرح بجواز أن يكون المحمول أخص من موضوع العلم لصحة إرجاعه إلى العرض الذاتي بالمفهوم المردد، فعدم اعتبار الأول وهو كون المحمول أعم من موضوع العلم والأعم منه مساوي الأقدام في جواز إرجاعه إلى العرض الذاتي له فاعتبار أحدهما دون الآخر ترجيح بلا مرجح تحكم أي دعوى بلا دليل بخلاف المبادي: فإنها أعم من أن تكون داخلة أو لا، فالمبادي أعم مطلقا من المقدمات، وعلى تفسير المقدمة بما يعين في تحصيل الفن تكون المقدمات أعم. (إسماعيل و أبو الفتح) بخلاف المبادي: فإنها لا يلزم أن تكون خارجة عن العلم؛ الجواز أن يكون ما يبدأ به قبل الشروع في مقاصد العلم داخلا في العلم على أنها أي الرؤوس الثمانية من المفهومات أو من المبادي بالمعنى الأعم لا بالمعنى الأخص؛ لأن الرؤوس الثمانية خارجة عن العلم ومتوقف عليها الشروع فيه على وجه البصيرة.
And it may be said: al-mabadi' (beginnings) are what is started with before the intended object, and al-muqaddimat (premises) are what the commencement depends upon in the way of insight and strong desire, like the definition of the science and the explanation of its goal and its subject. And the ancients used to mention at the beginning of the book what they call the eight heads: the first: the purpose; so that the inquiry in seeking it not be vain. So the teacher (al-Ustadh) explicitly considered the second, so not considering the first is arbitrary. And here there is additional speech that the place does not accommodate. His statement: 'And it may be said: al-mabadi' is an indication of another terminology in al-mabadi' besides what Ibn al-Hajib set forth in Mukhtasar al-Usul; for he applied al-mabadi' to what is begun with before commencing the aims of the science, whether it is internal to the science, then it is from the previous terminological mabadi' like the conception of the subject and the per se accidents and the assents that compose the syllogisms of the science, or external, upon which commencement depends even if by way of expertise, and they are called muqaddimat, like knowing the definition, goal, and subject. And the difference between muqaddimat and mabadi' in this sense is such that it should not be ambiguous; for muqaddimat are necessarily external to the science, unlike mabadi', so understand. His statement: 'they mention' i.e. at the beginning of their books, as being from the muqaddimat or from the mabadi' in the broader sense. His statement: 'the purpose: 'they mention at the beginning of the book' in all existing copies, manuscript and printed, except in an old correct manuscript where it is not present, and apparently it is correct; because the author is engaged in refining the speech and clarifying the commentator's saying 'at the beginning of their books' would be superfluous in this copy, so understand. Al-Ustadh: Jalal al-Ulama' explicitly permitted that the predicate be more specific than the subject of the science due to the validity of referring it to the per se accident by the disjunctive concept, so not considering the first, i.e. the predicate being more general than the subject of the science, and what is more general than it is equal in footing in the permissibility of referring it to the per se accident, so considering one without the other is a preponderance without a preponderator, arbitrary i.e. a claim without evidence. Unlike al-mabadi': for they are more general than being internal or not, so al-mabadi' are absolutely more general than al-muqaddimat, and on the interpretation of al-muqaddima as what helps in acquiring the art, al-muqaddimat are more general. (Isma'il and Abu al-Fath) Unlike al-mabadi': for they are not necessarily external to the science; it is permissible that what is begun with before commencing the aims of the science be internal to the science. And 'they' i.e. the eight heads are from among the concepts or from the mabadi' in the broader sense, not in the more specific sense; because the eight heads are external to the science and the commencement upon them depends in the way of insight.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage provides a definitional clarification of the terms mabadi' (beginnings) and muqaddimat (premises) in the context of the sciences. The author begins by presenting another terminology: mabadi' are what one starts with before the intended object, while muqaddimat are what the commencement depends upon in a way that provides insight and strong desire, such as defining the science, its goal, and its subject. Then he mentions that the ancients used to include in the preamble of their books what they called the 'eight heads', the first of which is the purpose, to avoid vain inquiry. He notes that al-Ustadh (Jalal al-Ulama') explicitly considered the second (i.e., the predicate being more specific than the subject of the science) as permissible, so not considering the first (the predicate being more general) is arbitrary. The author then explains that this statement refers to another terminology of mabadi' different from the one used by Ibn al-Hajib in his Mukhtasar al-Usul. Ibn al-Hajib applied mabadi' to what is started with before commencing the aims of the science, which could be internal to the science (like the conception of the subject, per se accidents, and assents composing syllogisms) or external (called muqaddimat, like knowing the definition, goal, and subject). The key difference: muqaddimat are always external to the science, while mabadi' can be internal or external, so mabadi' is more general. The passage also comments on a textual variant in the manuscripts. Finally, it returns to the earlier discussion about predicate generality, reiterating that the teacher (al-Ustadh) allowed predicates more specific than the science's subject via disjunctive concepts, and that considering one without the other is arbitrary. It concludes that the eight heads are considered mabadi' in the broader sense, not the specific sense, because they are external to the science.

The Four Parts of a Scientific Introduction
Commentary defines the first four parts of a scientific introduction: purpose, benefit, characteristic, and author.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four parts of a scientific introduction (gharad, manfa'a, sima, mu'allif) and explain the role of each in the traditional presentation of a science.

Core Idea
The introductory framework of a science is built on its purpose, benefit, characteristic, and author.

When you open any classical Islamic textbook, the first few pages follow a time-tested structure. The scholars who compiled these works knew that a student needs more than just content; they need context. This lesson unpacks the four parts of a scientific introduction as defined in our passage: the purpose (gharad), the benefit (manfa'a), the characteristic (sima), and the author (mu'allif). Understanding this structure will help you read any classical text with deeper insight.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Four Parts
From the passage, extract the four numbered parts: the second (benefit), third (characteristic), fourth (author). Note that the first part (purpose) is discussed separately earlier. List them in order.
The passage explicitly mentions 'al-thani', 'al-thalith', 'al-rabi'.
2
Understand the Benefit (al-manfa'a)
The benefit is that which all people naturally desire, motivating them to seek the science and endure hardship. It is the 'what's in it for me' for the student.
3
Understand the Characteristic (al-sima)
The characteristic is the title of the science. The commentator gives the example of logic (mantiq) derived from 'nutq' (speech), showing how the name summarises the science's subject.
The marginal note by Abu al-Fath adds that sima also defines the science by its specific property.
4
Understand the Author (al-mu'allif)
Mentioning the author calms the student's heart by establishing trust. Knowing the author's reliability is a standard introductory matter.
The marginal note says it ensures the reader's heart is assured to accept the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: المنفعة أي ما يتشوقه الكل طبعا لينشط في الطلب ويتحمل المشقة، والثالث: السمة: وهي عنوان العلم؛ ليكون عنده إجمال ما يفصله، والرابع: المؤلّف؛ ليسكن قلب المتعلم، اعلم أن ما يترتب على الفعل إن كان باعثا للفاعل على صدور ذلك الفعل منه يسمى غرضا وعلة غائية، وإلا يسمى فائدة ومنفعة وغاية. قالوا: أفعال الله تعالى لا تعلل بالأغراض وإن اشتملت علي غايات ومنافع لا تحصى، فكأن مقصود المصنف أن القدماء كانوا يذكرون في صدر كتبهم ما كان سببا حاملا على تدوين المدون الأول لهذا العلم، ثم يعقبونه بما يشتمل عليه منفعة ومصلحة يميل إليها عموم الطبائع إن كانت لهذا العلم منفعة ومصلحة سوى الغرض من الباعث للواضع الأول، وقد عرفت في صدر الكتاب أن الغرض والغاية من علم المنطق هو العصمة. فتذكره قوله والثالث السمة: السمة العلامة وكأنّ المقصود ههنا الإشارة إلى وجه تسمية العلم كما يقال : إنما سمى المنطق منطقا؛ لأن المنطق يطلق على النطق الظاهري: وهو التكلم، وعلى الباطني وهو إدراك الكليات وهذا العلم يقوي الأول ويسلك بالثاني مسلك السداد، فاشتق له اسم من النطق، فالمنطق إما مصدر ميمي بمعنى النطق، أطلق على العلم المذكور مبالغة في مدخليته في تكميل النطق حتى كأنه هو وإما اسم مكان كأنّ هذا العلم محل النطق ومظهره، وفي ذكر وجه التسمية إشارة إجمالية إلى ما يفصله العلم من المقاصد. قوله والرابع المؤلف: أي معرفة حاله إجمالا ؛ ليسكن قلب المتعلم على ما هو الشأن في مبادي الحال من معرفة حال الأقوال بمراتب الرجال. :وإلا أي وإن لم يكن باعثا للفاعل على صدور الفعل بعد ما يترتب عليه تسمى بهذه الأسامي الثلاثة: الفائدة والمنفعة والغاية. قالوا أي قالوا: بناء على الفرق بين الغرض والغاية والمنفعة والفائدة بأن أفعال الله تعالى إلخ. والثالث السمة وكان المقصود منه تعريف العلم برسمه وبيان خاصة من خواصه. (أبو الفتح) والرابع المؤلف : أي تعيين المؤلف ؛ ليطمئن قلب الشارع في قبول كلامه بالاعتماد عليه. (أبو الفتح)
The second: the benefit, i.e. that which all desire by nature, to become active in seeking and endure hardship. The third: the characteristic, which is the title of the science, so that he has a summary of what it details. The fourth: the author, to calm the student's heart. Know that what results from an act, if it is a motivator for the agent to produce that act, is called a purpose and final cause; otherwise it is called a benefit, advantage, or end. They said: The actions of God Most High are not caused by purposes, even though they include countless ends and benefits. So it is as if the author's intention is that the ancients used to mention at the beginning of their books what was a cause impelling the first compiler of this science, then they would follow it with what comprises benefit and interest that general natures incline to, if this science has a benefit and interest other than the purpose that motivated the first founder. And you have known at the beginning of the book that the purpose and end of the science of logic is infallibility. So remember his saying: The third: the characteristic: the characteristic is the sign, and it seems the intention here is to indicate the aspect of naming the science, as it is said: Logic is only called 'mantiq' because 'mantiq' is applied to external speech, which is speaking, and to internal speech, which is the apprehension of universals, and this science strengthens the first and directs the second along the path of correctness. So a name was derived for it from 'nutq' (speech). So 'mantiq' is either a verbal noun meaning speech, applied to the mentioned science as an exaggeration of its role in perfecting speech until it is as if it is it, or a place name, as if this science is the place and manifestation of speech. And in mentioning the aspect of naming there is a summary indication of what the science details of objectives. His saying: The fourth: the author: i.e. knowing his state in summary, to calm the student's heart, as is the custom in introductory matters of knowing the state of statements by the ranks of men. And otherwise: i.e. if it is not a motivator for the agent to produce the act, then what results from it is called by these three names: benefit, advantage, and end. They said: i.e. they said, based on the distinction between purpose and end and benefit and advantage, that the actions of God Most High etc. And the third: the characteristic: its intended purpose was to define the science by its delineation and to clarify one of its special properties. (Abu al-Fath) And the fourth: the author: i.e. specifying the author, so that the heart of the reader (sharih) may be assured in accepting his speech by relying on him. (Abu al-Fath)
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the parts of a scientific introduction. It defines the second, third, and fourth parts in detail, with explanation of key terms. The second part is al-manfa'a (benefit), which naturally motivates the student. The third part is al-sima (characteristic), which is the title of the science; the commentator explains the etymology of 'mantiq' (logic) from 'nutq' (speech), distinguishing external and internal speech. The fourth part is al-mu'allif (author), needed to give confidence to the student. The commentary also clarifies the distinction between gharad (purpose) and fa'ida (benefit): if the result motivates the agent, it is a purpose; otherwise it is a benefit. The commentator notes that God's actions are not motivated by purposes. The marginal notes by Abu al-Fath add that the characteristic defines the science by its specific property, and the author is specified for trust.

المنفعة
السمة
المؤلف
الغرض

D) Calming the student's heart

Key Takeaway
A scientific introduction is not arbitrary; it systematically presents the purpose, benefit, characteristic, and author to prepare the student's mind.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take any classical Islamic work you have studied (e.g., a fiqh text or a tafsir) and identify which of the four parts appear in its introduction. Write them down.
?
Explain why the author of a logic text would include the purpose (gharad) as 'infallibility in thought' and the benefit (manfa'a) as 'distinguishing truth from falsehood'.
